The Universal Proposition of Usul al-Fiqh and the Construction of Proof
Explains the qadiyya kulliya and its subdivisions as premises for istinbat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three subdivisions of the qadiyya kulliya and apply the Hanafi method of tarkib al-dalil to derive a fiqh ruling from usul premises.

Core Idea
The qadiyya kulliya in usul al-fiqh becomes the major premise linking proof to ruling.

This passage from al-Tawdih explains the structure and scope of usul al-fiqh through the concept of the universal proposition (qadiyya kulliya). It shows that the subject matter of usul encompasses proofs, rulings, acts, and subjects, and that these serve as premises in syllogistic reasoning to derive fiqh rulings. Understanding this framework is essential for grasping how Hanafi jurists construct legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three dimensions of the qadiyya kulliya
The passage divides the discussions into those related to the proof (dalil), those related to the ruled act (mahkum bih), and those related to the legal subject (mahkum alayh). Recognize that all three are encompassed by the universal proposition.
The proof (dalil) is the source; the mahkum bih is the act of the mukallaf; the mahkum alayh is the mukallaf himself.
2
Trace the construction of the proof (tarkib al-dalil)
The passage provides an example: the claim 'This ruling is established' is supported by a minor premise describing the ruling, act, capacity, and absence of accidents, and a major premise from usul al-fiqh. Follow this structure.
The minor premise includes specific attributes; the major premise is a general usul principle.
3
Distinguish between syllogism and mulazama
The passage explains that the major premise can be expressed as a universal conditional ('Whenever x, then y') in an exceptive qiyas (shartiyya muttasila). Note the difference between the categorical syllogism and the implicative form.
In mulazama, the usul principle becomes the implication, and the minor premise affirms the antecedent.
4
Recognize the role of usul al-fiqh
The passage concludes that the usul principles themselves (e.g., 'Every ruling with these attributes is valid') are the major premise or implication. Thus, usul al-fiqh provides the universal premises for deriving fiqh rulings.
This confirms that usul al-fiqh is a science of general proofs and general rulings.

قال المصنف في التوضيح هذا الذي ذكرنا انما هو بالنظر الى الدليل قضیہ کلیہ کے متعلق جو تفصیل ہم نے ذکر کی وہ بلحاظ دلیل کے تھی فاما بالنظر إلى المدلول يعني بنظر مدلول قضیہ کلیہ کا اثبات اس وقت ممکن ہے جب انواع احکام کی معرفت حاصل ہو اور اس کی معرفت ہو کہ کس قسم کا حکم کس نوع ادلہ سے حاصل ہوتا اور یہ خصوصیت ناشی من الحکم ہوتی ہے یعنی خصوصیت حکم کی بنا پر خصوصیت دلیل کا فیصلہ ہوتا ہے مثلاً ایک شی کسی حکم کے لئے علہ ہونا یہ ایک ایسا حکم ہے قیاس اس کا اثبات نہیں بلکہ یہ صرف نص کے ساتھ ہو گا ثمر المباحث المتعلقة بالمحكوم بہ اور وہ فعل مکلف ہے مثلاً اس فعل کا عبادت ہونا عقوبت ہونا اور اس جیسی اشیاء یہ بھی اس قضیہ کی کلیہ میں درج ہیں کیونکہ احکام افعال مکلفین کے اختلاف کی وجہ سے مختلف ہو جاتے ہیں خان العقوبات لا يمكن ايجابها ای اثباتها بالقياس ثو المباحث المتعلقة بالمحكوم علیہ اور وہ خود مکلف ہے۔ یعنی وہ مباحث جو خود مکلف انسان کے ساتھ متعلق ہیں مثلاً اس کی اہمیت کی معرفت اور ان عوارض کی بحث جو اس اہمیت پر طاری ہو جاتے ہیں خواہ یہ عوارض سماویہ ہوں جن میں انسان کے فعل اور اکتساب کو دخل نہیں ہوتا یا مکتبہ ہوں جن کے اندر انسانی عمل و اکتساب کو دخل ہوتا ہے یہ تمام مباحث اس قضیہ کلیہ کے تحت درج ہیں کیونکہ محکوم علیہ کے مختلف ہونے کی وجہ سے احکام میں اختلاف واقع ہو جاتا اور اسی طرح وجود عوارض اور قدم عوارض کے لحاظ سے بھی احکام مختلف ہو جاتے ہیں لہذا یہ تمام مباحث قضیہ کلیہ کے ساتھ متعلق ۔ فيكون تركيب الدلیل یعنی مسائل فقہ کے اثبات پر شکل اول کے ساتھ جب استدلال کریں گے تو ان تمام مباحث کو پیش نظر رکھنا ہو گا تو ترکیب دلیل اس طرح ہوگی - هذا الحكم ثابت یہ دعویٰ ہے دلیل اس کی یوں ہوگی لانه حکو هذا شانہ یہ انواع حکم کی طرف اشارہ ہے متعلق بفعل هذا شانه به مباحث متعلقة بالمحکوم به یعنی فعل مکلف کی طرف اشارہ مثلاً اس کا عبادت ہونا هذا الفعل صادر من مكلف هذا شانہ یہ اہلیت مکلف کی طرف اشارہ ہے ولم يوجد العوارض الخ یہ عوارض طاریہ کی نفی کی جارہی ہے ويدل على ثبوت هذا الحكواة یہ نوع دلیل کا تعین ہے هذا هى الصغری یعنی یہاں تک صغری مکمل ہوا ہے۔ ثو الكبری یعنی کبری دلیل کا یوں ہوگا کل حکو موصوف اتم تو نتیجہ ہو گا هذا الحکم ثابت فهذه القضية الاخيرة یعنی جو کبری اہلیہ اصول فقہ کے مسائل میں سے ہے و بطريق الملازمة یعنی اگر بطریق ملازمہ یعنی قیاس استثنائی کے طور پر استدلال کریں تو یوں ہوگا كلما وجد قیاس موصوف الا اور بطور استثناء کے کہیں گے لکنه وجد القياس الموصوف الخ تو نتیجہ يثبت هذا الحكم آئے گا تو كلما وجد سے جو ملازمہ پیش کیا گیا ہے یہ مسائل اصول فقہ سے ہے اور یہی مراد ہے توصل قریب سے یعنی اصول فقہ کا قضیہ ہماری دلیل کا ایک مقدمہ ہو جائے۔ فعلم ان جميع المباحث الج یعنی یہ تمام مباحث مذکورہ متقدمہ اس قضیہ کلیہ کے تحت درج ہیں جو دلیل کے لئے ایک مقدمہ بنتا ہے یعنی کبری یا ملازمہ کلیہ تو جب یہ مباحث مذکورہ بعض متعلقہ بالدلیل اور بعض بالحکم ہیں تو معلوم ہوا کہ اس علم میں یعنی اصول فقہ میں ادلہ شرعیہ کلیہ اور احکام کلیہ سے بحث کی جاتی ہے۔ من حيث ان الاولى الى الاول مثبتة للثانية الى الاحكام تو بعض مباحث ناشی من الادلہ ہوں گے اور بعض من الاحکام تو جب ادلہ اور احکام کے عوارض ذاتیہ سے بحث ہوئی تو معلوم
The author said in al-Tawdih: 'This that we have mentioned is only with respect to the proof' - the detail we mentioned was with respect to the proof. As for with respect to the indicated (madlul), meaning by considering the indicated, the establishment of the general proposition is possible when there is knowledge of the types of rulings and knowledge of which type of ruling is derived from which type of proof. And this specificity arises from the ruling, i.e., the specificity of the ruling determines the specificity of the proof. For example, a thing being a cause for a ruling is a ruling that qiyas cannot establish; rather it is only through text. Then the discussions related to the mahkum bih (the ruled act) - and that is the act of the mukallaf, e.g., the act being an act of worship or punishment and similar things - these are also included in this general proposition, because rulings vary due to the difference of acts of mukallafin. The punishments cannot be made obligatory, i.e., established, by qiyas. Then the discussions related to the mahkum alayh (the subject) - and that is the mukallaf himself, i.e., the discussions related to the human mukallaf, such as knowledge of his capacity (ahliyya) and the discussion of the accidents (awarid) that befall this capacity, whether they are heavenly accidents in which human action and acquisition have no part, or acquired ones in which human action and acquisition have a part. All these discussions come under this general proposition, because rulings differ due to the difference of the mahkum alayh, and similarly rulings differ with respect to the existence and absence of accidents. Therefore all these discussions relate to the general proposition. So the composition of the proof (tarkib al-dalil) - i.e., when we argue for the establishment of the massā'il of fiqh using the first figure, we must keep all these discussions in view. So the composition of the proof will be as follows: 'This ruling is established' is the claim. Its proof will be: 'Because it is a ruling of such description' - this indicates the types of rulings. 'Related to an act of such description' - this points to the discussions of the mahkum bih, i.e., the act of the mukallaf, e.g., it being an act of worship. 'This act is issued from a mukallaf of such capacity' - this indicates the capacity of the mukallaf. 'And there exist no accidents' - this negates temporary accidents. 'And there is evidence for the establishment of this ruling' - this is the specification of the type of proof. This is the minor premise. Then the major premise will be: 'Every ruling with these attributes' - so the conclusion will be 'This ruling is established'. This last proposition - i.e., the major premise - is from the massā'il of usul al-fiqh. And by the method of mulazama (implication), i.e., if we argue by the method of implication (exceptive qiyas), it will be: 'Whenever there is a qiyas with these attributes, except...' and by way of exception we say: 'But this qiyas with these attributes exists', so the conclusion 'This ruling is established' follows. So the 'whenever' that presents the implication is from the massā'il of usul al-fiqh, and this is what is meant by 'near arrival' (tawassul qarib), i.e., the proposition of usul al-fiqh becomes a premise of our evidence. So it is known that all the aforementioned discussions come under this general proposition that serves as a premise for the proof - i.e., the major premise or the general implication. So when these mentioned discussions are some related to the proof and some related to the ruling, it becomes known that in this science (usul al-fiqh) one discusses the general sharia proofs and the general rulings. From the aspect that the first (proofs) are the means of establishing the second (rulings). So some discussions arise from the proofs and some from the rulings. So when the essential accidents of the proofs and rulings are discussed, then it is known...
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the scope of usul al-fiqh. It explains that the general proposition (qadiyya kulliya) in usul al-fiqh encompasses three types of discussions: those related to the proof (dalil), those related to the ruling (hukm) and the act subject to the ruling (mahkum bih), and those related to the legal subject (mahkum alayh) – the mukallaf himself. The author then demonstrates how these discussions serve as premises in a syllogism to derive fiqh rulings. Using the first figure of Aristotelian logic, he constructs a demonstrative argument: the minor premise describes the specific ruling, act, capacity, and absence of obstructions; the major premise is a general principle from usul al-fiqh (e.g., 'every ruling with these attributes is established by the Shafi'i'? Actually it's a generic example). The conclusion is the fiqh ruling. Alternatively, using implicative reasoning (mulazama), the usul principle forms the universal conditional, and its application yields the conclusion. This shows that usul al-fiqh provides the premises (major premise or implication) for deriving fiqh rulings. The text emphasizes that all these discussions are essential components of the qadiyya kulliya, thereby defining the subject matter of usul al-fiqh as the study of the general proofs and general rulings of sharia.

Key Terms
قضية كلية (qadiyya kulliya): A general proposition that encompasses all discussions in usul al-fiqh related to proofs, rulings, and subjects.
محكوم به (mahkum bih): The act of the mukallaf that is the object of the ruling.
محكوم عليه (mahkum alayh): The subject (mukallaf) who bears the ruling.
تركيب الدليل (tarkib al-dalil): The construction of the logical proof using a minor and major premise to derive a fiqh ruling.
ملازمة (mulazama): Implicative conditional used in exceptive qiyas to argue that whenever the antecedent holds, the consequent holds.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the construction of the proof (tarkib al-dalil), what role does the general proposition of usul al-fiqh play?
A) It is the minor premise describing the specific facts.
B) It is the major premise or the implicative conditional.
C) It is the conclusion that is being proved.
D) It is the legal basis for the ruling.

Key Takeaway
The universal proposition of usul al-fiqh is a comprehensive premise that, when combined with specific attributes of a case, yields a fiqh ruling through syllogistic or implicative reasoning.

Three Types of Essential Accidents in Usul al-Fiqh
Classifies essential accidents into three types for both adillah and ahkam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three categories of essential accidents for proofs (adillah) and rulings (ahkam) and apply the classification to given examples.

Core Idea
Essential accidents classify attributes: directly investigated, indirectly affecting, or irrelevant to usul al-fiqh.

This passage introduces a foundational classification in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: essential accidents ('awarid dhatiyyah) that pertain to proofs (adillah) and rulings (ahkam). The author divides them into three precise categories, each playing a distinct role in the science. Understanding this classification helps the student organize the vast array of attributes discussed in usul and recognize which ones are central, which are auxiliary, and which are extraneous.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types for adillah
Read the passage and list the three categories of essential accidents for proofs: (1) those investigated in usul, e.g., 'being established for rulings'; (2) those not investigated but affecting the investigated ones, e.g., 'amm (general), mushtarak (equivocal), khabar wahid (solitary report); (3) those neither investigated nor affecting, e.g., being eternal or temporal.
2
Apply the same division to ahkam
Observe that the same three-part division applies to rulings: (1) being proven by the proofs; (2) being related to acts of a mature person vs. a child, or being devotional vs. penal; (3) entirely irrelevant attributes.
3
See how these function in propositions
The passage explains that the first type forms the predicate (mahmul) in scientific propositions, the second type qualifies the subject (subject attributes or restrictions), and the third type is excluded from discussion entirely. Trace examples like 'al-'amm yujibu al-hukm qat'an' where 'al-'amm is the subject and 'yujibu al-hukm qat'an' is the predicate relying on the first-type accident.

And know that the essential accidents are three types: first, those that are investigated in this science, such as adillah being established for ahkam; second, those not investigated but involved in the occurrence of investigated accidents, such as 'amm and mushtarak and khabar wahid; third, those that are neither investigated nor involved, such as adillah being qadim or hadith. Similarly for ahkam: first, being proven by adillah; second, related to acts of adult or child; third, neither investigated nor involved. The first type become predicates in propositions of this science; the second become attributes of subjects; the third are not discussed.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage defines the essential accidents ('awarid dhatiyyah) of both proofs (adillah) and rulings (ahkam) in usul al-fiqh. The commentator explains that these accidents are divided into three categories: (1) those directly investigated in the science, such as the quality of 'being established for rulings' for proofs; (2) those not directly investigated but which affect the investigated accidents, like 'amm (general), mushtarak (equivocal), or khabar wahid (solitary report); (3) those entirely irrelevant, like a proof being eternal or temporal. The same division applies to rulings: investigated (e.g., being proven by the proofs), affecting (e.g., relating to a mature person vs a child), and irrelevant. The passage then illustrates how these accidents function in the propositions of the science: the first type forms the predicate, the second type serves as qualifiers for the subject, and the third type is excluded from discussion entirely.

Key Terms
عارض ذاتی (aarid dhati): An attribute that pertains to the essence of a thing and is inseparable from it in the mind of the scholar.
عام ('amm): A word that applies to all individuals of its category without restriction.
خبر واحد (khabar wahid): A report transmitted by a single or limited chain that does not reach the level of tawatur.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many types of essential accidents exist for both adillah and ahkam?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Essential accidents in usul al-fiqh fall into exactly three categories: directly investigated, indirectly affecting, and irrelevant.

Using Essential Attributes in Usul Masail: The Four Types of Predication and Scope of Usul al-Fiqh
Explaining how essential attributes are used in masail and why Quran/Sunnah are not discussed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of predication of essential attributes in usul masail and explain why the hujjiyya of Quran and Sunnah is not discussed in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Usul al-fiqh only investigates non-self-evident proofs; the authority of Quran and Sunnah is taken as given from kalam.

This passage clarifies how the essential attributes of proofs and rulings are structured into the issues (masail) of usul al-fiqh. It describes four distinct modes of predication and then addresses a fundamental question: why are ijma' and qiyas discussed as proofs, while the Quran and Sunnah are not? The answer lies in the self-evident nature of the primary sources after their establishment in kalam. Understanding this scope is essential for grasping the subject matter of usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of predication (haml)
The commentator lists four ways an essential attribute (arad dhatiyya) can be predicated: on the subject itself, on a type of the subject, on another essential attribute, and on a type of an essential attribute. Locate each example in the text and note the subject and predicate.
Examples: (1) 'the Book establishes the ruling' – attribute on subject; (2) 'the command indicates obligation' – attribute on a type; (3) 'the general indicates certainty' – attribute on another attribute; (4) 'the partially specified general indicates probability' – attribute on a type of attribute.
2
Understand the scope of masail in usul al-fiqh
The text explains that all discussions ultimately revolve around establishing the essential attributes of proofs and rulings, either from the side of affirmation (ithbat) or being affirmed (thubut). Recognize that each mas'ala has a subject (mawdu') and a predicate (mahmul) drawn from the essential attributes.
The predicate is always an essential attribute of either the proof or the ruling.
3
Analyze the question and answer about exclusion of Quran and Sunnah
The passage poses a question: why are the Quran and Sunnah not discussed as proofs in usul, while ijma' and qiyas are? The answer contrasts self-evident (badihi) vs. non-self-evident (kasbi) matters. Locate the explanation and note the criteria for inclusion.
The hujjiyya of Quran and mutawatir Sunnah is considered settled in kalam; usul only takes it as a given.
4
Apply the classification to other proofs
The text also mentions anomalous readings (qira'a shadhdha) and solitary reports (khabar al-wahid) as non-self-evident, hence discussed. This shows the pattern: any proof whose authority is debatable is included.
This clarifies the boundary between usul and kalam.

And what is meant by 'investigation' is that we will use these attributes in the issues as a matter of course. When they become predicates (mahmul), what will be the subject of the issue? The commentator has detailed this and explained the four types of subject of the issue. First: predicating them on the subject of the science (hamlaha 'ala mawdu' al-'ilm). That is, the essential attributes will be predicated on the subject itself. So in an issue, the predicate is an essential attribute and the subject of the issue is the subject of the science itself, e.g., 'the Book establishes the ruling definitively.' Here, establishing the ruling is among the attributes of the proofs, so it has been made into a predicate, and 'the Book' is the subject. Second: on its types (ala anwa'ihi) – i.e., predicating the essential attribute on a type within the subject of the science. For example, 'the command indicates obligation' – 'command' is a type of the Book, and 'indicates obligation' is an essential attribute that is the predicate. Third: on its essential attributes (ala a'radihi al-dhatiyya) – i.e., predicating an essential attribute on another essential attribute of the subject of the science. For example, 'the general (al-amm) indicates certainty' – here 'indicates certainty' is an essential attribute of the proof, and it is predicated on 'general', which is also an essential attribute. Fourth: on the types of its attributes (ala anwa'i a'radihi) – i.e., predicating an essential attribute on a type of essential attribute. For example, 'the general that has been partially specified indicates probability' – here 'indicates probability' is an essential attribute predicated on 'the general that has been partially specified', which is a type of the absolute general. All discussions of usul al-fiqh are directed toward establishing the essential attributes of the proofs and rulings for themselves. From the perspective of establishing the proofs (min haythu ithbat al-adilla) – i.e., that the proofs are affirmers of rulings, and the rulings are affirmed by proofs. That is, the function of proofs is affirmation, and the rulings are what are affirmed. Meaning all predicates of this science are issues of affirmation and of being affirmed, and those that have a role in this affirmation and being affirmed and are beneficial in this regard are also predicate issues. So its subject is: the subject of the science is the proofs and rulings – the proofs insofar as they affirm the rulings, and the rulings insofar as they are affirmed by the proofs. If you say: what is the matter? The summary of this question is: what is the reason that ijma' and qiyas being affirmers of rulings is included in the issues of usul al-fiqh and discussed, while the affirming nature of the Book of Allah and the Sunnah of His Messenger (peace and blessings be upon him) is not included in the issues nor discussed? The answer: the summary of the reply is that in a science, the main objective is acquired matters (kasbiyyat), i.e., those issues that are theoretical and require a proof for their establishment; self-evident matters are not included in the objectives of the science. Since the probative nature of the Book and Sunnah is, in the view of the usuli, akin to self-evident matters, having been established and settled in the science of kalam and widely accepted among the people, it is therefore not included among the discussed issues of usul al-fiqh. In contrast, the probative nature of ijma' and qiyas being affirmers of rulings was not self-evident, so it has been included among the discussed issues. Similarly, the probative nature of anomalous readings (qira'a shadhdha) of the Book of Allah and of khabar al-wahid (solitary reports) from the Sunnah was not clear and self-evident, so the usuli scholars have addressed this issue and discussed it. His statement: 'As for the third' – i.e., the third type of attributes, whether of the proofs or the rulings, which are not discussed in this science and have no role in attaching to what is discussed – they are our...
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage explains how the essential attributes (arad dhatiyya) of proofs and rulings are used in the masail (issues) of usul al-fiqh. The commentator describes four types of predication (haml) that determine the subject of an issue: 1) predicating the attribute on the subject itself (e.g., 'the Book establishes the ruling definitively'), 2) on a type of the subject (e.g., 'the command indicates obligation'), 3) on another essential attribute (e.g., 'the general indicates certainty'), and 4) on a type of an essential attribute (e.g., 'the general that has been partially specified indicates probability'). All discussions in usul al-fiqh revolve around establishing these attributes. The passage then addresses a critical question: why are the Quran and Sunnah themselves not discussed as proofs in usul, while ijma' and qiyas are? The answer is that the probative nature (hujjiyya) of the Quran and mutawatir Sunnah is self-evident (badihi) to the usuli, having been established in kalam, so they are not debated. In contrast, the authority of ijma', qiyas, anomalous readings, and khabar al-wahid is not self-evident, thus requires investigation. This explains the scope of usul al-fiqh: it deals only with non-self-evident proofs and the derived rulings.

Key Terms
الحمل (al-haml): Predication; the relation between the subject (mawdu') and predicate (mahmul) in a proposition. Here, it refers to attributing an essential attribute to the subject of the science, its types, or other attributes.
المسألة (al-mas'ala): An issue or proposition in usul al-fiqh consisting of a subject and predicate, typically attributing an essential characteristic to a proof or ruling.
البدیهی (al-badihi): Self-evident; a proposition that requires no proof and is immediately known. In this text, the hujjiyya of Quran and Sunnah is considered badihi after being established in kalam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a type of predication of essential attributes?
A) Predicating on the subject of the science
B) Predicating on a type of the subject
C) Predicating on a subsidiary attribute
D) Predicating on a type of an essential attribute

Key Takeaway
The four types of predication structure every mas'ala in usul al-fiqh, and the discipline only investigates proofs whose authority is not self-evident, leaving the hujjiyya of Quran and Sunnah to kalam.

Essential Accidents and the Scope of Usul al-Fiqh
Defining the subject of usul al-fiqh and its essential accidents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the essential accidents that form the subject matter of usul al-fiqh and distinguish between self-evident and acquired proofs based on their role in the science.

Core Idea
Usul al-fiqh only discusses attributes of proofs and rulings that are essential to establishment and being established.

This passage defines the scope of usul al-fiqh by focusing on its subject: proofs and rulings. Not every attribute of a proof is relevant—only those that are 'essential accidents' like generality or probability. The text also explains why some proofs like ijma' and qiyas are debated while others like Qur'an and Sunnah are assumed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
Determine what two things usul al-fiqh studies according to the passage: proofs (adilla) and rulings (ahkam). The passage states that the subject is proofs in the capacity of establishing rulings, and rulings in the capacity of being established by proofs.
2
Classify predicates
Recognize that the predicates (mahmulat) discussed in usul al-fiqh are of two categories: establishment (ithbat) and being established (thubut). Only attributes that fall under these are relevant.
This is derived from the statement that all predicates are from the category of ithbat or thubut.
3
Apply the carpenter analogy
Understand the third type of accidents: those that have no involvement in ithbat or thubut (e.g., possibility, composition, linguistic features). The analogy of the carpenter shows that only attributes relevant to the craft (establishment of rulings) are discussed.
4
Distinguish badihi from acquired
The passage explains why the authoritativeness of Qur'an and Sunnah is not discussed: it is self-evident (badihi) and already proven in kalam. In contrast, ijma', qiyas, and certain types of reports are not self-evident, so they require discussion.

Like our statement: 'The general term indicates certainty' — here, 'indicates certainty' which is an essential accident of the proof is being predicated on 'the general term', and 'generality' is also an essential accident. And 'upon the types of its accidents' — this is the fourth type, i.e., predication of an essential accident upon a species of an essential accident, like our statement: 'The general term from which some parts have been specified indicates probability' — here, 'indicates probability' as an essential accident is being predicated on 'the specified general term', which is a species of absolute general term. All discussions of usul al-fiqh, i.e., all debates of the science of usul al-fiqh, revert to this: that the essential accidents of the proofs and rulings are established for them. In terms of the establishment of the proofs, i.e., in the capacity that proofs are establishers of rulings, and rulings in the capacity that they are established by proofs, i.e., the function of proofs is establishment and of rulings is being established. Meaning that all predicates, i.e., all predicated matters of this science, will be from the category of establishment and from the category of being established, and also those matters that have an involvement in this establishment and being established and are beneficial in this regard will also be predicated matters. So its subject will be the proofs and the rulings — proofs in the capacity that they establish rulings, and rulings in the capacity that they are established by proofs. If you say: What is the matter with them? — the gist of this question is: What is the reason that the fact that ijma' and qiyas are establishers of rulings is entered into the issues of usul al-fiqh and discussed, while the fact that the Book of Allah and the Sunnah of the Messenger (peace be upon him) are establishers of rulings is neither entered into the issues nor discussed? I say: The gist of the answer is that in a science, the primary objective is acquired matters, i.e., those issues that are theoretical and in need of proof for their establishment. Obvious matters and issues are not included in the objectives of the science. Since the authoritativeness of the Book and the Sunnah, in the view of the usuli, is like self-evident matters that have been established and confirmed in kalam and have gained widespread acceptance among people, therefore it was not included among the discussed issues of usul al-fiqh. Contrary to ijma' and qiyas, that their being authoritative and establishers of rulings was not self-evident, so it was included among the discussed issues. Similarly, the recitations of the Qur'an that are shadhdh and the khabar wahid from the Sunnah, their being establishers of rulings was not apparent and self-evident, so the usuli scholars addressed this issue and discussed it. His statement: 'And as for the third' — i.e., the third type of accidents, whether they belong to proofs or rulings, which are not discussed in this science nor do they have any involvement in the attachment of the discussed matters — they are completely separate from our science and will not be discussed. For example: possibility, pre-eternity, temporal origination, simplicity, composition of the proof; its being a nominal sentence or a verbal sentence; its individual components being triliteral or quadriliteral; being inflected or indeclinable. These have no involvement either in establishment or in being established. Such accidents will not be discussed in usul al-fiqh because they are not related to our science. Like a carpenter who works with wood: he pays attention to the hardness and softness of the wood, its thinness and thickness, its crookedness and straightness, because these matters are related to his craft. As for matters not related to his craft, for example, the wood's possibility, its being originated, its composition and simplicity, he pays no attention to these at all. The author said in al-Tawdih: 'And annexed to it... the discussion of what is established by these proofs, which is the ruling, and of what is related to it.' The author, analyzing this expression, said: 'Annexed to it' — the pronoun in 'it' is referent to 'discussion', which the word 'fijut' indicates. And his statement: 'of what is established' — before the word 'ma' there is an implicit genitive construct, meaning 'of the states of what is established', and 'what is established' means 'the ruling', so it becomes 'of the states of the ruling'. 'Of what is related to it' — the pronoun in 'it' refers back to 'the ruling', i.e., those things that are related to the ruling, and they are the hakim (the legislator), the mahkum bih (the thing ruled upon), and the mahkum alayh (the subject of the ruling). Know that his statement 'and annexed to it' admits two possibilities. That is, the discussion of the ruling being annexed to the discussion of proofs is subject to two meanings. First, one meaning could be that the discussions of the ruling are mentioned after the primary discussions, because firstly the proof is prior in essence relative to the ruling, and secondly the discussion of proofs in usul al-fiqh is more important than the discussion of rulings. Therefore, the primary discussions should be given precedence and the discussions of rulings should be delayed, with the understanding that the subject of this science is both, i.e., proofs and rulings are both the subject of the science, but the discussion of rulings being annexed and delayed means only a delay in discussion. Second, the other possibility is: the gist of it is that the subject of this science is only the proofs; rulings are not the subject of the science. However, rulings are discussed in the capacity that they are annexed and subsidiary discussions of this science of usul al-fiqh, not the primary objective. Because usul al-fiqh is called 'the proofs of fiqh', but the intended meaning is knowledge of the proofs in the capacity that they are establishers of rulings. So the primary objective is knowledge of the proofs, and those discussions that arise from the ruling and its related matters are external to the objectives of this science, and their mention is only to the extent that they are followers and adjuncts of the issues of the science. And such issues are also few in number, just as the subject...
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on the definition of the subject matter (mawdu') of usul al-fiqh. It begins by discussing the essential accidents (a'rad dhatiyya) that are investigated in this science: the predicates (mahmulat) must be either establishment (ithbat) by proofs or being established (thubut) by proofs. The author then addresses a possible objection: why are ijma' and qiyas discussed in usul al-fiqh while the Qur'an and Sunnah are not? The answer is that the authoritativeness of the Qur'an and Sunnah is self-evident (badihi) and already proven in kalam, while ijma' and qiyas require proof. Similarly, shadhdh recitations and khabar wahid are not self-evident and thus are discussed. Next, the passage explains the third type of accidents that are not discussed at all because they have no relevance to the science's purpose, using the analogy of a carpenter (sannaj) who only considers attributes of wood relevant to his craft. Finally, the text examines the statement from al-Tawdih that discussion of rulings is 'annexed' (mulhaq) to the discussion of proofs. Two interpretations are given: (1) both proofs and rulings are the subject, but rulings come later; (2) only proofs are the true subject, and rulings are merely subsidiary. This elucidates the scope of usul al-fiqh, emphasizing that its primary focus is proofs (adilla) in their capacity as establishers of rulings.

Key Terms
عرض ذاتی (arad dhati): An essential accident that is predicated on the subject of the science; in usul al-fiqh, these are things like 'generality' (umum) or 'probability' (zann) that attach to proofs or rulings.
بدیہی (badihi): Self-evident truths that do not require proof within the science, such as the authoritativeness of the Qur'an and Sunnah.
الحاقی (ilhaqi): Annexed or subsidiary discussions that are not the primary subject of the science, such as the discussion of rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the passage say that ijma' and qiyas are discussed in usul al-fiqh while the Qur'an and Sunnah are not?
A) Because ijma' and qiyas are more important sources.
B) Because the authoritativeness of Qur'an and Sunnah is self-evident.
C) Because ijma' and qiyas are stronger proofs.
D) Because the Qur'an and Sunnah are discussed in fiqh instead.

Key Takeaway
Usul al-fiqh studies essential accidents of proofs and rulings relevant to establishment, leaving aside self-evident and irrelevant attributes.

Method of Learning
intermediate
6 min
The Annexation of Rulings: Two Interpretations
Analyzing the annexation of rulings to proofs in usul al-fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two interpretations of the annexation of rulings in usul al-fiqh and state the practical consequence for the subject matter of the science.

Core Idea
Whether rulings are subject proper or merely annexed changes the emphasis of usul al-fiqh.

The passage from al-Tawdih states that discussion of rulings is 'annexed' (mulhaq) to discussion of proofs. This word admits two interpretations: one treating both proofs and rulings as subject, with rulings coming later; another treating only proofs as the true subject, with rulings as subsidiary. Understanding this clarifies the scope and priority in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the annexed discussion
Locate the statement in the passage: 'قال المصنف في التوضيح ويلحق به البحث عما يثبت بهذه الادلة وهو الحكم'. This shows that the discussion of rulings is annexed to the discussion of proofs.
The pronoun in 'ylhaq bihi' refers to the discussion (al-bahth) of proofs.
2
First interpretation: both are subject
One interpretation: the subject includes both proofs and rulings, but rulings are mentioned after proofs due to proofs' priority in essence and importance. Here 'ilhaq' means delay (ta'khir) in presentation.
3
Second interpretation: only proofs are subject
Another interpretation: the true subject is only the proofs; rulings are not part of the subject but are annexed subsidiary discussions. 'Ilhaq' here means addition or appendage, not full inclusion.
4
Evaluate the implications
Consider how each interpretation affects the scope of usul al-fiqh. The first expands the subject to include rulings; the second narrows it to proofs only. The passage notes that rulings are 'few in number' and subsidiary, leaning toward the second view.

گیا ۔ بخلاف الاجماع و القیاس کہ ان کا حجت مثبته للاحکام ہونا بدیہی نہیں تھا اس لئے اس کو مسائل محجوبہ میں درج کر لیا گیا ہے اور اسی طرح کتاب اللہ کی قرآة شاذة اور سنت میں سے خبر واحد کا مثبت للاحکام کے مفردات کا ثلاثی اور رباعی ہونا۔ معرب اور مبنی ہونا جن کو نہ دخل فی الاثبات نہ فی الثبوت ایسے عوارضات اصول فقہ میں زیر بحث نہیں آئیں گے کیونکہ یہ ہمارے فن کے ساتھ متعلق نہیں ہیں جیسے سنجار جو لکڑی به يحتمل امرین یعنی بحث حکم کو جو بحث اور کے ساتھ ملحق قرار دیا گیا ہے یہ دو معانی کا محتمل ہے احدهما ان یراد اتم ایک مراد تو اس کی یہ ہوسکتی ہے کہ مباحث حکم کو مباحث اولہ کے بعد ذکر کیا جائے کیونکہ ایک تو دلیل مقدم بالذات ہوتی ہے نسبت حکم کے دوسرا یہ کہ اصول فقہ میں بحث ادلہ زیادہ اہم ہے بنسبت بحث احکام کے لہذا مباحث اولہ کو مقدم کرنا چاہیئے اور مباحث احکام اس سے موخر ہوں علی ان موضوع الم یعنی Like our statement: 'The general term indicates certainty' — here, 'indicates certainty' which is an essential accident of the proof is being predicated on 'the general term', and 'generality' is also an essential accident. And 'upon the types of its accidents' — this is the fourth type, i.e., predication of an essential accident upon a species of an essential accident, like our statement: 'The general term from which some parts have been specified indicates probability' — here, 'indicates probability' as an essential accident is being predicated on 'the specified general term', which is a species of absolute general term. All discussions of usul al-fiqh, i.e., all debates of the science of usul al-fiqh, revert to this: that the essential accidents of the proofs and rulings are established for them. In terms of the establishment of the proofs, i.e., in the capacity that proofs are establishers of rulings, and rulings in the capacity that they are established by proofs, i.e., the function of proofs is establishment and of rulings is being established. Meaning that all predicates, i.e., all predicated matters of this science, will be from the category of establishment and from the category of being established, and also those matters that have an involvement in this establishment and being established and are beneficial in this regard will also be predicated matters. So its subject will be the proofs and the rulings — proofs in the capacity that they establish rulings, and rulings in the capacity that they are established by proofs. If you say: What is the matter with them? — the gist of this question is: What is the reason that the fact that ijma' and qiyas are establishers of rulings is entered into the issues of usul al-fiqh and discussed, while the fact that the Book of Allah and the Sunnah of the Messenger (peace be upon him) are establishers of rulings is neither entered into the issues nor discussed? I say: The gist of the answer is that in a science, the primary objective is acquired matters, i.e., those issues that are theoretical and in need of proof for their establishment. Obvious matters and issues are not included in the objectives of the science. Since the authoritativeness of the Book and the Sunnah, in the view of the usuli, is like self-evident matters that have been established and confirmed in kalam and have gained widespread acceptance among people, therefore it was not included among the discussed issues of usul al-fiqh. Contrary to ijma' and qiyas, that their being authoritative and establishers of rulings was not self-evident, so it was included among the discussed issues. Similarly, the recitations of the Qur'an that are shadhdh and the khabar wahid from the Sunnah, their being establishers of rulings was not apparent and self-evident, so the usuli scholars addressed this issue and discussed it. His statement: 'And as for the third' — i.e., the third type of accidents, whether they belong to proofs or rulings, which are not discussed in this science nor do they have any involvement in the attachment of the discussed matters — they are completely separate from our science and will not be discussed. For example: possibility, pre-eternity, temporal origination, simplicity, composition of the proof; its being a nominal sentence or a verbal sentence; its individual components being triliteral or quadriliteral; being inflected or indeclinable. These have no involvement either in establishment or in being established. Such accidents will not be discussed in usul al-fiqh because they are not related to our science. Like a carpenter who works with wood: he pays attention to the hardness and softness of the wood, its thinness and thickness, its crookedness and straightness, because these matters are related to his craft. As for matters not related to his craft, for example, the wood's possibility, its being originated, its composition and simplicity, he pays no attention to these at all. The author said in al-Tawdih: 'And annexed to it... the discussion of what is established by these proofs, which is the ruling, and of what is related to it.' The author, analyzing this expression, said: 'Annexed to it' — the pronoun in 'it' is referent to 'discussion', which the word 'fijut' indicates. And his statement: 'of what is established' — before the word 'ma' there is an implicit genitive construct, meaning 'of the states of what is established', and 'what is established' means 'the ruling', so it becomes 'of the states of the ruling'. 'Of what is related to it' — the pronoun in 'it' refers back to 'the ruling', i.e., those things that are related to the ruling, and they are the hakim (the legislator), the mahkum bih (the thing ruled upon), and the mahkum alayh (the subject of the ruling). Know that his statement 'and annexed to it' admits two possibilities. That is, the discussion of the ruling being annexed to the discussion of proofs is subject to two meanings. First, one meaning could be that the discussions of the ruling are mentioned after the primary discussions, because firstly the proof is prior in essence relative to the ruling, and secondly the discussion of proofs in usul al-fiqh is more important than the discussion of rulings. Therefore, the primary discussions should be given precedence and the discussions of rulings should be delayed, with the understanding that the subject of this science is both, i.e., proofs and rulings are both the subject of the science, but the discussion of rulings being annexed and delayed means only a delay in discussion. Second, the other possibility is: the gist of it is that the subject of this science is only the proofs; rulings are not the subject of the science. However, rulings are discussed in the capacity that they are annexed and subsidiary discussions of this science of usul al-fiqh, not the primary objective. Because usul al-fiqh is called 'the proofs of fiqh', but the intended meaning is knowledge of the proofs in the capacity that they are establishers of rulings. So the primary objective is knowledge of the proofs, and those discussions that arise from the ruling and its related matters are external to the objectives of this science, and their mention is only to the extent that they are followers and adjuncts of the issues of the science. And such issues are also few in number, just as the subject...
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on the definition of the subject matter (mawdu') of usul al-fiqh. It begins by discussing the essential accidents (a'rad dhatiyya) that are investigated in this science: the predicates (mahmulat) must be either establishment (ithbat) by proofs or being established (thubut) by proofs. The author then addresses a possible objection: why are ijma' and qiyas discussed in usul al-fiqh while the Qur'an and Sunnah are not? The answer is that the authoritativeness of the Qur'an and Sunnah is self-evident (badihi) and already proven in kalam, while ijma' and qiyas require proof. Similarly, shadhdh recitations and khabar wahid are not self-evident and thus are discussed. Next, the passage explains the third type of accidents that are not discussed at all because they have no relevance to the science's purpose, using the analogy of a carpenter (sannaj) who only considers attributes of wood relevant to his craft. Finally, the text examines the statement from al-Tawdih that discussion of rulings is 'annexed' (mulhaq) to the discussion of proofs. Two interpretations are given: (1) both proofs and rulings are the subject, but rulings come later; (2) only proofs are the true subject, and rulings are merely subsidiary. This elucidates the scope of usul al-fiqh, emphasizing that its primary focus is proofs (adilla) in their capacity as establishers of rulings.

Key Terms
ملحق (mulhaq): Annexed; something that is attached or appended to the primary subject. In this passage, discussion of rulings is mulhaq to discussion of proofs.
الحاکم (al-hakim): The legislator (Allah) who is the source of rulings.
محکوم به (mahkum bih): The thing ruled upon, i.e., the act or object of the ruling.
محکوم علیه (mahkum alayh): The subject of the ruling, i.e., the person who is legally addressed (mukallaf).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main difference between the two interpretations of annexation (ilhaq)?
A) One considers rulings as subject, the other does not.
B) One says rulings are proofs, the other says they are not.
C) One considers the discussion of rulings as prior, the other as posterior.
D) One is from al-Tawdih, the other is from the commentator.

Key Takeaway
The annexation of rulings to proofs can mean either that rulings are part of the subject but discussed later, or that they are merely subsidiary additions to the primary focus on proofs.

Method of Learning
intermediate
8 min
Are Rulings Part of Usul al-Fiqh's Subject Matter?
Analyzing whether rulings belong to the subject matter of usul al-fiqh or are merely appendices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two interpretations of 'wa yulhaqu bihi' regarding rulings in usul al-fiqh and explain why the Hanafi school considers rulings as part of the subject matter but studied after proofs.

Core Idea
Rulings are part of usul's subject matter but studied after proofs, as they are the effect of proofs.

This passage from al-Tawdih examines a foundational question: are the rulings (ahkam) themselves part of the subject matter of usul al-fiqh, or are they merely appendages? The author presents two interpretations and defends the first, with a nuanced discussion of how 'proof' (ithbat) applies to rulings. Understanding this helps clarify the scope and structure of usul al-fiqh as a science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations
The passage presents two probabilities for the phrase 'wa yulhaqu bihi' regarding rulings. State both interpretations concisely.
Interpretation 1: rulings are part of the subject but studied after proofs. Interpretation 2: only proofs are the subject; rulings are appendages.
2
Analyze the reasoning for each
For each interpretation, note the justification given: priority of proofs by essence and importance vs. defining usul as knowledge of proofs only.
The first interpretation emphasizes that proofs are prior and more important; the second limits the subject to proofs.
3
Determine the correct view
The passage explicitly states 'al-sahih huwa al-ihmal al-awwal'. Identify the textual basis and the supporting argument (analogy to logic).
The correct view is that rulings are part of the subject, but discussed later.
4
Examine the nuance about hukm and ithbat
The passage distinguishes between hukm as divine address (qadim) and as effect (obligation/prohibition). Understand how ithbat applies differently: from Quran/Sunnah/Ijma, ithbat establishes certainty; from Qiyas, it establishes preponderance of opinion.
This nuance resolves a potential contradiction about how rulings are 'proved'.
5
Address the objection and refutation
An objection is raised about combining literal and metaphorical usage in 'ithbat'. Follow how the passage refutes it by redefining 'proof' as establishing either certainty or preponderance, and 'knowledge' as including both.
The refutation shows that ithbat is used in a single sense (establishing awareness), avoiding the need for metaphorical interpretation.

Know that his statement 'and appended to it' can be understood in two ways. First: that the discussions of rulings are mentioned after the primary discussions, because the proof is prior by essence to the ruling, and because discussions of proofs are more important. Thus they are both part of the subject matter, but rulings are discussed later. Second: that the subject of this science is only proofs; rulings are not part of it but are discussed as appendages. The correct view is the first. If by ruling is meant the address of Allah, it is eternal and its proof from the evidences means our knowledge of it. If by ruling is meant the effect of the address (obligation, prohibition), then its proof from some evidences is correct, but not from analogy because analogy only establishes preponderance of opinion. If it is objected that this involves combining literal and metaphorical usage, we respond that 'proof' here means establishing knowledge or preponderance of opinion for us, and 'knowledge' includes certainty and preponderance.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage discusses whether the study of rulings (ahkam) is part of the subject matter of usul al-fiqh or merely an appendix. The author presents two interpretations of the phrase 'wa yulhaqu bihi' from al-Tawdih. The first interpretation holds that rulings are part of the subject, but studied after proofs due to priority and importance. The second holds that only proofs are the subject, and rulings are discussed only as appendices. The correct view is the first. The passage then addresses a nuance: if ruling means the actual divine address, it is eternal and our knowledge of it is derived from proofs. If it means the effect (obligation, prohibition), then proof from Quran, Sunnah, Ijma is valid, but from Qiyas it only establishes preponderance of opinion. The objection of combining literal and metaphorical usage is refuted by redefining 'proof' as establishing either certainty or preponderance, and 'knowledge' as including both.

Key Terms
الحكم (al-hukm): The divine address concerning the actions of the mukallaf, or its effect (obligation, prohibition).
الاثبات (al-ithbat): Establishing knowledge or preponderance of opinion from the proofs.
الموضوع (al-mawdu'): The subject matter of a science; what is primarily discussed in it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the two probabilities is considered correct (sahih)?
A) The first probability: rulings are part of the subject matter, studied after proofs.
B) The second probability: only proofs are the subject, rulings are merely appendices.
C) Both are equally valid and there is no preferred view.
D) Neither; the author does not express a preference.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school considers rulings part of usul's subject matter but studied after proofs, reflecting the priority of proofs over their effects.

The Subject Matter of Usul al-Fiqh: Proofs and Rulings
Dispute over subject matter of Usul al-Fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on the subject matter of Usul al-Fiqh, evaluate the opposing view, and explain why rulings are integral to the discipline.

Core Idea
Usul al-Fiqh studies both proofs and rulings, not proofs alone.

Every discipline has a subject matter (mawdu') that defines its scope. In Usul al-Fiqh, a classical debate centers on whether rulings (ahkam) are part of that subject or merely subsidiary. This passage, drawn from the commentary tradition, defends the Hanafi view that both proofs (adillah) and rulings are essential. Understanding this dispute clarifies how Usul al-Fiqh is structured and why discussions about the nature of rulings are not peripheral but central to the discipline.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the contested question: What is the subject matter of Usul al-Fiqh? Some scholars restrict it to proofs; others, including the Hanafi school, include rulings.
The issue is about the scope of the discipline itself.
2
Identify the Hanafi position and its basis
The correct view (al-sahih) holds that both proofs and rulings are the subject. The basis is twofold: an analogy with logic, where both conveyors and objects are discussed, and the observation that actual Usul texts discuss states of rulings (e.g., classification into five types).
The analogy with logic is a key methodological argument.
3
Examine the opposing view
Sahib al-Ahkam (likely al-Amidi) argues that only the four proofs are the subject; rulings are merely conceptualized to facilitate understanding of proofs, not analyzed in themselves.
This view limits the discipline's scope.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
If rulings were not part of the subject, discussing their states (e.g., obligation, prohibition) would be extraneous. Yet such discussions are essential in Usul. The definition of Usul as 'universal propositions by which one arrives at fiqh' necessarily includes both proofs and rulings.
The rebuttals focus on the coherence and completeness of the discipline.
5
Determine the practical consequence and the correct view
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is the scope of Usul: whether it includes discussions on the nature of rulings. The tarjih (preferred view) is the Hanafi position, which is more comprehensive.
This dispute influences which topics are considered core in Usul.

المنطق یہ احتمال ثانی کی نظیر ہے یعنی جیسے منطق کا موضوع تصورات اور تصدیقات ہیں بایں حیثیت کہ یہ موصل الی تصویر و تصدیق ہیں تو موضوع منطق موصل تصوری اور موصل تصدیقی ہوا اس لئے منطق کے بیشتر مسائل احوال موصل کے متعلق ہیں لیکن علی سبیل الندرة والقلة تصور موصل الیہ کی بحث کی جاتی ہے مثلاً یہ کہ ماہیات قابل حد ہیں تو اس قسم کی بحث مقاصد فن سے نہیں ہوتی بلکہ صرف تبعا ذکر کی جاتی ہے اسی طرح اصول فقہ میں حکم کی بحث صرف تبعا ہے بعض کتب اصول میں مباحث حکم کو اصول فقہ کے مباحث سے شمار نہیں کیا گیا ۔

قال الشارح في التلويج قوله كما ان موضوع المنطق الخ یعنی جیسے موضوع منطق تصورات و تصدیقات ہیں بایں حیثیت کہ یہ موصل ہیں تو منطق میں مقصودہ مباحث احوال موصل یا ان امور کے احوال جن کو دخل فی الایصال ہے جیسے تصورات میں احوال تصور سے بحث کی جاتی ہے کہ یہ حد ہے اور یہ رسم ہے جو کہ ایصال الى التصور کرتی ہے یہ خود احوال موصل کی بحث ہوئی یا منطق میں یہ بحث کی جاتی ہے یہ جنس ہے یا فصل وخاصہ تو جنس و فصل سے ترکیب حد ہوتی ہے اور خاصہ رسم کے حاصل کرنے میں کام دیتا ہے تو یہ ایسے مباحث ہیں کہ جنہیں دخل فی الایصال ہے اور اسی طرح تصدیقات میں احوال تصدیق سے بحث کی جاتی ہے بایں حیثیت کہ یہ حجت ہے جو مجہول تصدیقی کی طرف موصل ہے یا یہ بحث کی جاتی ہے کہ یہ قضیہ ہے یہ عکس قضیہ اور نقیض قضیہ ہے ان سے حجت کی تالیف و ترکیب ہوتی ہے بہر حال منطق کے جمیع مباحث راجع ہیں احوال موصل کی طرف یا ان امور کے احوال کی طرف جنہیں دخل فی الایصال ہے اور کبھی احوال موصل الیہ سے تبعاً بحث کی جاتی ہے لیکن یہ مقاصد فن اور مسائل فن سے نہیں ہوتے مثلا جیسے یہ بحث کی جاتی ہے کہ اگر ماہیت بسیطہ ہو تو اس کی حد نہیں ہو سکتی اگر مركب من الجنس والفصل ہو تو اس کی حد کی جائے گی اگر اس کے لئے خاصہ لازمہ بلینہ ہو تو رسم ہوگی والا فلا یہ تمام مباحث بالقبع ہیں نہ بالقصد
ويمكن ان يجعل یہ ان مقاصد تبعیہ کو مقاصد اصلیہ میں داخل کرنے کی توجیہ ہے حاصل یہ کہ ان مباحث کو راجع الی احوال الموصل کیا جا سکتا ہے یوں کہا جائے کہ مراد یہ ہے کہ حد موصل الی المرکب ہوگی نہ الی البسیط تو اس توجیہ کے ساتھ یہ مباحث مسائل فن سے ہو جائیں گے ۔
قوله لكن الصحيح - ذهب صاحب الاحكام الم [؟] اصول فقہ کے موضوع میں اختلاف ہے جس کی طرف مصنف نے اشارہ کر دیا ہے صاحب الاحکام ادھر چلے گئے ہیں کہ اصول فقہ کا موضوع صرف ادلہ اربعہ ہیں اور احکام موضوع فن نہیں ہیں لہذا احوال احکام سے بحث نہیں کی جائے گی بلکہ صرف ان کے تصور کر لینے کی ضرورت ہوتی ہے تاکہ ان کے اثبات اور نفی پر تمکن اور قدرت حاصل ہو سکے یعنی یہ معلوم ہو سکے کہ کس قسم کے احکام کس نوع ادلہ سے ثابت ہو سکتے ہیں لکن صحیح لیکن صحیح بات یہ ہے کہ اصول فقہ کا موضوع ادلہ اور احکام دونوں ہیں۔ لانا رجعنا یہ دونوں کے موضوع ہونے کی دلیل ہے حاصل یہ کہ ادلہ کا بالعموم رجوع الی الاربعہ ہے اور احکام کا الی الخمسہ وجوب - ندب - حرمت - کہ اہت۔ اباحت پھر ہم نے اجمالاً ان مباحث کی طرف نگاہ کی جو اس امر کے ساتھ متعلق ہیں کہ اثبات الادلہ الا حکام کی کیا کیفیت ہے یعنی ادلہ جو احکام کو ثابت کرتی ہیں ان کی کیفیت اور طریق کار کیا ہے تو ہم نے بعض مباحث کار جوع احوال ادلہ کی طرف اور بعض کا رجوع احوال احکام کی طرف محسوس کیا جیسے کہ مصنفہ قضیہ کلیہ جس کے ساتھ توصل الی الفقہ ہوتا ہے اس کی تفصیل و تحصیل کرتے ہوئے ذکر کیا ہے جب دونوں کے احوال کے ساتھ مباحث متعلق ہیں تو پھر ایک کو موضوع قرار دے کر مقاصد فن سے شمار کرنا اور دوسرے کو صرف لواحق و توابع میں درج کرتا تحکم ہے۔ غایت مافی الباب بس اتنا ہے کہ مباحث ادلہ اکثر اور اہم ہیں
Logic is similar to this second probability, i.e., just as the subject of logic is concepts and judgments insofar as they are means of conveyance to conception and affirmation, so the subject of logic becomes means of conveyance to conception and means of conveyance to affirmation. Therefore most issues of logic pertain to the states of the means of conveyance, but occasionally and rarely the conception of the object of conveyance is discussed, for example, whether essences are capable of definition. Such discussion is not from the objectives of the discipline but is mentioned only as a subsidiary. Similarly, in Usul al-Fiqh, the discussion of rulings is only subsidiary; some books of Usul do not count discussions of rulings among the discussions of Usul al-Fiqh. But the correct view is the first probability, i.e., that ruling is also a subject of the discipline and its discussions are included among the intended discussions of Usul al-Fiqh, but discussions of rulings come after discussions of proofs. If what is meant by 'ruling' is the address of Allah related to [؟], then it is eternal and its establishment from the proofs necessitates its temporality. So what is meant by 'establishment of the ruling' is the establishment of our knowledge of the ruling, and our knowledge of the ruling is established from proofs, not the ruling itself, because the ruling is eternal. And if what is meant by 'ruling' is not the address but the effect of the address, i.e., obligation, prohibition, etc., then its establishment from some proofs, i.e., the Book, Sunnah, and consensus, is correct, but with analogy it is not correct, because analogy does not establish obligation; rather, with analogy a preponderance of opinion of obligation is established, as it has been said that analogy is a revealer, not an establisher. So the intended meaning of 'establishment' is the establishment of preponderance of opinion. And if objected, in this case there is a combination of literal and metaphorical usage, because the word 'establishment' is one: the establishment of proofs for rulings. So in some proofs, the establishment of knowledge of the ruling was intended, and in others, the establishment of preponderance of opinion of the ruling was intended, and combining literal and metaphorical usage is not correct. We respond that in all proofs, the intended meaning of 'establishment' is the establishment of knowledge for us or the establishment of preponderance of opinion for us, i.e., at that time, 'knowledge' means absolute perception, whether certain or preponderant. Thus, in this respect, the word 'knowledge' includes both certain and probable. The commentator said in al-Talwih: 'His statement: just as the subject of logic...' i.e., just as the subject of logic is concepts and judgments insofar as they are conveyors, then the intended discussions in logic are the states of the conveyors or the states of those matters that have a role in conveyance, such as in concepts: the states of concept are discussed: that this is a definition (hadd) and this is a description (rasm), which effect conveyance to conception—this itself is a discussion of the states of the conveyor; or in logic, this is discussed: this is a genus or a differentia or a specific property; from genus and differentia a definition is composed, and the specific property assists in obtaining the description. So these are discussions that have a role in conveyance. Similarly, in judgments, the states of judgment are discussed insofar as it is an argument that conveys to the unknown judgment, or it is discussed: this is a proposition, this is the converse of a proposition, and this is the contradictory of a proposition; from these, the composition and structure of the argument occurs. In any case, all discussions of logic refer to the states of the conveyor or to the states of those matters that have a role in conveyance. Sometimes the states of the object of conveyance are discussed subsidiarily, but these are not among the objectives of the discipline or its issues, for example, the discussion: if an essence is simple, it cannot be defined; if it is composed of genus and differentia, it will be defined; if it has a necessary clear specific property, it will have a description, etc. All these discussions are by way of following, not by intention. And it is possible to make this a justification for including these subsidiary objectives into the primary objectives. The conclusion is that these discussions can be referred back to the states of the conveyor by saying: what is meant is that definition conveys to the composite, not to the simple. With this justification, these discussions become issues of the discipline. His statement: 'But the correct [view]'—Sahib al-Ahkam went to [the view] that the subject of Usul al-Fiqh is only the four proofs, and rulings are not the subject of the discipline. Therefore, the states of rulings are not discussed; rather, only their conceptualization is needed so that ability and power to establish or negate them can be obtained, i.e., it can be known which types of rulings can be established from which types of proofs. But the correct view is that the subject of Usul al-Fiqh is both proofs and rulings. 'Because we refer'—this is the evidence for both being the subject. The conclusion is that proofs generally refer to the four, and rulings to the five: obligation, recommendation, prohibition, dislike, permissibility. Then we have, in summary, looked at those discussions that are related to how proofs establish rulings, i.e., what is the quality and method of proofs that establish rulings. So we have observed that some discussions refer to the states of proofs and some to the states of rulings, as the author mentioned while detailing and achieving the universal proposition by which one arrives at fiqh. When discussions are related to the states of both, then making one the subject and counting it among the objectives of the discipline while placing the other only among accessories and appendices is arbitrary. The utmost in the matter is that discussions of proofs are more numerous and important.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a dispute over the subject matter (mawdu') of Usul al-Fiqh. The author begins by drawing an analogy to logic (mantiq). In logic, the primary subject is the 'conveyors' (musil): concepts and judgments insofar as they lead to conception and affirmation. Discussions about the conveyors themselves are the main issues; discussions about the objects of conveyance (like whether essences are definable) are subsidiary. Similarly, some scholars hold that in Usul al-Fiqh, only the proofs (adillah) are the subject, and rulings (ahkam) are only discussed subsidiarily. However, the correct view (al-sahih) is that both proofs and rulings are part of the subject, but discussions of rulings come after those of proofs. The author then clarifies a potential ambiguity: when we say 'establishment of rulings from proofs,' if ruling means the eternal address of Allah, then its 'establishment' actually means the establishment of our knowledge of the ruling, not the ruling itself. If ruling means the effect (like obligation), then it can be established by certain proofs (Qur'an, Sunnah, ijma) but not by qiyas (analogy), because qiyas only produces preponderant opinion (ghalabat al-zann), not certainty. To avoid combining literal and metaphorical usage of the word 'ithbat' (establishment) across proofs, the author explains that 'knowledge' in this context includes both certain and probable perception, so the word 'ithbat' can apply uniformly to both types of proofs. The commentator in al-Talwih expands on the logic analogy, detailing how all discussions in logic ultimately relate to the states of the conveyors. He also offers a justification to include subsidiary discussions (like whether a simple essence can be defined) into the primary discussions by showing they can be seen as relating to the conveyor. On page 143, the author mentions that Sahib al-Ahkam (possibly al-Amidi or another) held that the subject of Usul is only the four proofs. But the correct view is that both proofs and rulings are subject, as evidenced by the fact that discussions in Usul al-Fiqh cover states of both proofs and rulings. The author emphasizes that arbitrarily excluding one and including the other is unjustified. The conclusion is that discussions of proofs are more numerous and important but both are integral.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawdu'): The subject matter or object of study of a discipline; that which the discipline primarily investigates.
اثبات (ithbat): Establishment or proof; in this context, the process by which a ruling is derived or confirmed from a proof.
موصل (musil): That which conveys or leads to something; in logic, concepts and judgments are conveyors to conception and affirmation; in Usul, proofs are conveyors to rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view presented in this passage, what constitutes the subject matter of Usul al-Fiqh?
A) Only the four proofs (Kitab, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas)
B) Only the rulings (ahkam)
C) Both proofs and rulings
D) Only the universal propositions

Key Takeaway
The subject of Usul al-Fiqh includes both the proofs (adillah) and the rulings (ahkam) they establish; both are essential for deriving fiqh.

Subject Matter of Usul al-Fiqh: Both Adillah and Ahkam
Explaining that Usul al-Fiqh includes both the four proofs and the five rulings as its subject matter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the subject matter of Usul al-Fiqh and evaluate the opposing view from Al-Ahkam.

Core Idea
Usul al-Fiqh studies both the proofs (adillah) and the rulings (ahkam); one without the other is incomplete.

This passage clarifies a fundamental methodological dispute in Usul al-Fiqh: what exactly is the subject matter of this discipline? While some limit it to the four proofs, the Hanafi school insists that rulings themselves must also be studied. Understanding this dispute shapes how you approach the entire field and highlights the Hanafi emphasis on the practical goal of deriving fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing positions
Locate where the author contrasts the view of the author of Al-Ahkam (only proofs) with the Hanafi correct view (both proofs and rulings). Note the key terms: 'ادله اربعه' and 'احکام خمسه'.
The opposing school is associated with al-Amidi in his work Al-Ahkam.
2
Understand the proof for each position
The opponent argues that rulings are merely conceptual tools needed to understand proofs. The Hanafi response argues that both are referenced in usul discussions; it would be arbitrary to exclude rulings.
The Hanafi proof is based on observation: usul discussions cover conditions of both proofs and rulings.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the opposing view
The author calls the exclusion of rulings 'arbitrary' (تحكم). He notes that while proof discussions may be more numerous, that does not make rulings subordinate.
This is a methodological argument about the logical scope of the discipline.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In the Hanafi school, usul includes the study of the five rulings (obligation, recommendation, etc.) and their conditions. In the opposing view, these are left to fiqh or logic.
This affects how usul texts are structured and what topics they cover.

قوله لكن الصحيح - ذهب صاحب الأحكام إلى أن اصول فقہ کے موضوع میں اختلاف ہے جس کی طرف مصنف نے اشارہ کر دیا ہے صاحب الاحکام ادھر چلے گئے ہیں کہ اصول فقہ کا موضوع صرف ادلہ اربعہ ہیں اور احکام موضوع فن نہیں ہیں لہذا احوال احکام سے بحث نہیں کی جائے گی بلکہ صرف ان کے تصور کر لینے کی ضرورت ہوتی ہے تاکہ ان کے اثبات اور نفی پر تمکن اور قدرت حاصل ہو سکے یعنی یہ معلوم ہو سکے کہ کس قسم کے احکام کس نوع ادلہ سے ثابت ہو سکتے ہیں لکن صحیح لیکن صحیح بات یہ ہے کہ اصول فقہ کا موضوع ادلہ اور احکام دونوں ہیں۔ لانا رجعنا یہ دونوں کے موضوع ہونے کی دلیل ہے حاصل یہ کہ ادلہ کا بالعموم رجوع الی الاربعہ ہے اور احکام کا الی الخمسہ وجوب، ندب، حرمت، کراہت، اباحت پھر ہم نے اجمالاً ان مباحث کی طرف نگاہ کی جو اس امر کے ساتھ متعلق ہیں کہ اثبات الادلہ للاحکام کی کیا کیفیت ہے یعنی ادلہ جو احکام کو ثابت کرتی ہیں ان کی کیفیت اور طریق کار کیا ہے تو ہم نے بعض مباحث کا رجوع احوال ادلہ کی طرف اور بعض کا رجوع احوال احکام کی طرف محسوس کیا جیسے کہ مصنفہ قضیہ کلیہ جس کے ساتھ توصل الی الفقہ ہوتا ہے اس کی تفصیل و تحصیل کرتے ہوئے ذکر کیا ہے جب دونوں کے احوال کے ساتھ مباحث متعلق ہیں تو پھر ایک کو موضوع قرار دے کر مقاصد فن سے شمار کرنا اور دوسرے کو صرف لواحق و توابع میں درج کرنا تحکم ہے۔ غایت مافی الباب بس اتنا ہے کہ مباحث ادلہ اکثر اور اہم ہیں اور مباحث احکام قلیل المقدار ہیں یہ اس کا متقاضی نہیں ہے کہ ایک کو یعنی ادلہ کو اصل مستقل قرار دیدیویں اور احکام کو غیر مقصود اور صرف تابع قرار دیدیویں بلکہ دونوں اصالۃ اور استقلالاً موضوع فن ہیں۔
This works in doing, so these are discussions that have entry in the means of communication. And similarly, in the case of tasdiqat, the conditions of tasdiq are discussed in that it is a proof that leads to an unknown tasdiq, or it is discussed that this is a proposition, this is the converse of a proposition and the contradictory of a proposition; from these the composition and structure of proofs is formed. In any case, all discussions of logic refer to the conditions of the means or to the conditions of those matters that have entry in the means of communication. And sometimes discussions of the conditions of the thing to which the means leads are conducted incidentally, but these are not among the objectives and issues of the discipline. For example, it is discussed that if the essence is simple, then its definition is not possible; if it is composed of genus and differentia, then its definition will be made; if it has a necessary specific property, then a description will be made; etc. All these discussions are secondary, not primary.

It can be made that this is an explanation for including these secondary objectives into the primary objectives. The upshot is that these discussions can be referred to the conditions of the means. One can say that the intent is that a definition leads to the composite, not to the simple. So with this explanation, these discussions become issues of the discipline.

His statement: 'but the correct [view]' — the author of Al-Ahkam went to that there is a difference in the subject of Usul al-Fiqh, to which the author has pointed. The author of Al-Ahkam went in that direction that the subject of Usul al-Fiqh is only the four proofs, and rulings are not the subject of the discipline. Therefore, discussions of the conditions of rulings will not be conducted, but only their conceptualization is needed so that one can have capability and power over their affirmation and negation, i.e., it can be known which types of rulings can be proven by which type of proofs. But the correct [view] is that the subject of Usul al-Fiqh is both proofs and rulings. 'Because we have referred' — this is the proof of both being the subject. The upshot is that proofs generally refer to the four, and rulings refer to the five: obligation, recommendation, prohibition, dislike, permission. Then we briefly looked at those discussions that are related to what the quality of proving rulings by proofs is, i.e., what is the quality and method of proofs that prove rulings. So we felt that some discussions refer to the conditions of proofs and some refer to the conditions of rulings, as the author mentioned while detailing and providing the universal proposition by which reaching fiqh is achieved. When discussions are related to the conditions of both, then making one the subject and counting it among the objectives of the discipline while placing the other merely among appendages and dependencies is arbitrary. The most that can be said is that discussions of proofs are more numerous and important, and discussions of rulings are few in quantity. This does not necessitate that we make one, i.e., proofs, an independent principle and make rulings unintended and merely subordinate; rather, both are originally and independently the subject of the discipline.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a dispute regarding the subject matter of Usul al-Fiqh. The author begins by discussing how logical discussions can be incorporated into usul by referring them to the conditions of the means (al-muwassil) that lead to the rulings, thereby making them part of the discipline. This is a methodological point to justify the inclusion of such topics. Then he addresses the main dispute: the author of Al-Ahkam (probably al-Amidi) claimed that the subject of Usul al-Fiqh is only the four proofs (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas), and that rulings (ahkam) are not part of the subject; they are only needed as conceptual tools for understanding proofs. The correct view (al-sahih) according to the Hanafi tradition is that both proofs and rulings constitute the subject. The reasoning is that the purpose of usul is to derive fiqh rulings from proofs, so both the proofs and the rulings themselves must be studied. The author gives an example: proofs refer to the four, rulings refer to the five (wajib, mandub, haram, makruh, mubah). Since discussions must cover both the conditions of proofs and the conditions of rulings, both are integral. The fact that proof discussions are more numerous does not make rulings subordinate. This establishes the Hanafi position that Usul al-Fiqh studies both adillah and ahkam, not just the former.

Key Terms
ادلہ اربعہ (adillah arba'ah): The four primary sources of Islamic law: Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas
احکام خمسہ (ahkâm khamsah): The five legal values: wajib, mandub, haram, makruh, mubah
موصل (muwassil): That which leads to the goal; in usul context, the means by which fiqh rulings are derived
قضیہ کلیہ (qadiyya kulliyya): A universal proposition that serves as the principal means to derive fiqh

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view in the Hanafi tradition, what is the subject matter of Usul al-Fiqh?
A) Only the four proofs (adillah arba'ah)
B) Only the five rulings (ahkam khamsah)
C) Both the proofs and the rulings
D) Only the Quran and Sunnah

Key Takeaway
Usul al-Fiqh according to the Hanafi tradition studies both the legal proofs (adillah) and the rulings (ahkam) derived from them; this distinguishes it from other approaches that limit the discipline to proofs only.

Multiplicity of Subjects in Usul al-Fiqh
Discussing whether a single science can have multiple subjects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi criterion for a science having multiple subjects and apply it to determine that both adilla and ahkam are subjects of usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
A science can have multiple subjects only if it studies a relation between things.

This passage tackles a foundational question: what is the subject matter of usul al-fiqh? It argues that both the evidences (adilla) and the rulings (ahkam) are independent subjects because usul studies the relation between them. This resolves the apparent contradiction that a single science might have multiple subjects, by introducing the criterion of relationality (idafa).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Assertion
The passage opens by stating that both adilla and ahkam are originally and independently the subject of usul al-fiqh, not one subsidiary to the other.
This counters any view that reduces the subject to only evidence.
2
Recognize the Initial Objection
The text from al-Tawdih is quoted: 'This is not correct' — rejecting the idea of multiple subjects. Medicine is cited as a counterexample where some wrongly claim multiple subjects.
The matn appears to insist on a single subject for any science.
3
Apply the Relational Criterion
The commentator introduces the key: if a science investigates a relation (idafa) between things, then the relata can both be subjects. In usul al-fiqh, the relation is 'evidences establish rulings'.
This is the Hanafi investigation (al-tahqiq).
4
Analyze the Consequence
Since the relation involves both adilla and ahkam, properties (awarid) arise from each. Thus both are subjects. The unity of the science resides in the relation itself.
This preserves the unity of the science while allowing multiple subjects.
5
Contrast Non-Relational Sciences
If a science does not study a relation, it can have only one subject. For example, physics studies bodies, not a relation between distinct relata.
The exception confirms the rule.
