قال المصنف في التوضيح واعلماني لما وقعت في مباحث الموضوع الخ مصنف نے موضوع اور مسائل کے متعلق مباحث ثلثہ پیش کئے ہیں اگر چہ اس فن کے ساتھ متعلق تو نہیں لیکن چونکہ محصیل اور طالب علم ان مباحث سے مستغنی نہیں ہو سکتا اس لئے یہ مباحث بغرض افادہ پیش فرما دیئے۔ منها انهم قد ذكر و ان العلم الواحد الم یعنی علماء نے ذکر کیا ہے اور ان میں یہ امر شہرہ یافتہ ہے کہ قد يكون له اكثر من موضوع واحد یعنی ایک علم کے موضوع ایک سے زیادہ ہو سکتے ہیں یعنی علم ایک ہو اور موضوع اس کے متعدد ہوں یہ صحیح ہے کا لطب مثلاً جیسے علم الطب ایک علم ہے لیکن موضوع اس کے متعدد ہیں کیونکہ اس علم کے اندر بدنِ انسان کے احوال سے بحث کی جاتی ہے اور اسی طرح احوال ادویہ وغیرہ سے بھی بحث کی جاتی ہے تو اس فن کے دو موضوع ہیں ایک بدن انسان دوسرا ادویہ اس لئے ان دونوں کے احوال زیر بحث آتے ہیں مصنف نے اس کے متعلق فرمايا هذا غير صحیح کہ یہ بات تعدد موضوع والی صحیح نہیں۔ والتحقیق یعنی اس مسئلہ میں تحقیق حقیق یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی علم کے اندر اضافہ شئی الی آخر سے بحث کی جائے جیسے اصول فقہ میں کہ اس کے اندر اثبات الادلة للحکم یعنی اولہ کا حکم کو ثابت کرنا یہ زیر بحث آتا ہے اور جیسے منطق میں کہ ایصال تصور الى تصور وايصال تصديق إلى تصديق سے بحث کی جاتی ہے تو اس صورت میں وہ عوارض جن کو مبحوث عنہ کے ساتھ دخل اور تعلق ہے ان میں سے بعض عوارض ناشی عن احد المضافین ہیں اور بعض عوارض دوسرے سے ناشی ہیں جیسے اصول فقہ میں کہ بعض عوارض ناشی عن الادلہ ہیں اور بعض عن الحکم اور منطق میں بعض ناشی عن الموصل ہیں اور بعض عن الموصل الیہ تو اس صورت میں دونوں مضافین موضوع ہوں گے اصول فقہ کا موضوع ادلہ اور احکام دونوں ہیں و ان لم يكن المبحوث عنه الاضافة التم اگر اس فن میں مبحوث عنہ اضافت نہیں ہے تو ایسے علم واحد کے لئے اشیاء کثیرہ موضوع نہیں ہو سکتیں ۔ لان اتحاد العلمالم یعنی دلیل ہے اس کی کہ علم واحد کے لئے تعدد موضوع نہیں ہو سکتا حاصل
And the discussions of rulings are few in quantity. This does not require that we consider one, i.e., the evidences, as the principal independent (subject) and the rulings as non-intended and merely subsidiary. Rather, both are, originally and independently, the subject of the science. The author said in al-Tawdih: 'Know that when I came to the discussions of the subject...' The author presented three discussions related to the subject and issues, although they are not related to this science, but since the investigator and student cannot be independent of these discussions, he presented these discussions for benefit. Among them is that they have mentioned, and it is well-known, that a single science can have more than one subject, i.e., one science with multiple subjects. This is correct, as in medicine: the science of medicine is one science, but its subjects are multiple; it discusses the states of the human body and also the states of medicines, so it has two subjects: the human body and medicines. The author said regarding this: 'This is not correct' — i.e., the matter of multiple subjects is not correct. The investigation is: if a science investigates the relation of one thing to another — like usul al-fiqh where it investigates the establishment of evidences for rulings, and like logic where it investigates the delivery of a concept to a concept and a judgment to a judgment — then the properties (awarid) that pertain to the subject of investigation, some arise from one of the two relata and some from the other. In usul al-fiqh, some properties arise from the evidences and some from the ruling; in logic, some arise from that which conveys and some from that to which conveyed. In such a case, both relata are subjects. The subject of usul al-fiqh is both evidences and rulings. And if the subject of investigation is not a relation, then for a single science, many things cannot be subjects, because the unity of a science is only by the unity of its subject.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage begins by asserting that both adilla (evidences) and ahkam (rulings) are independent subjects of usul al-fiqh, not one subsidiary to the other. It then introduces a discussion from the matn (al-Tawdih) about whether a single science can have multiple subjects. The matn initially rejects the idea, citing medicine as an example where some wrongly claim multiple subjects. However, the commentator (sharh) provides a nuanced investigation: If a science studies the relation (idafa) between things, then the relata can both be subjects. In usul al-fiqh, the relation studied is 'establishment of evidences for rulings'; therefore, both evidences and rulings are subjects. If no relation is studied, then multiple subjects are not possible. This analysis resolves the apparent conflict: technically, the subject of usul al-fiqh is the relation itself, but its relata are considered subjects in a derivative sense. This is a classic Hanafi usul position integrating both the sources (adilla) and the derived rulings (ahkam) as integral parts of the science.

Key Terms
اضافة (idafa): Relation; when a science studies the relation between two things, both are subjects.
موضوع (mawdu'): The subject matter of a science; what is investigated about its properties.
عوارض (awarid): Properties or accidental attributes that are investigated in a science.
مضاف (mudaf): One of the two relata in a relation; each mudaf can be a subject.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the analyzed passage, what is the criterion that allows a single science to have more than one subject?
A) The science must be very comprehensive.
B) The science must investigate the relation between two things.
C) The science must be approved by the Hanafi school.
D) The science must have both a theoretical and practical part.

Key Takeaway
A science can legitimately have more than one subject if it studies the relation between those subjects, as usul al-fiqh does with evidences and rulings.

Can a Single Science Have Multiple Subjects? – Usul al-Fiqh Principle on Unity of Science
Debate on whether a single science can have more than one subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that the unity of a science derives from the unity of its subject, and identify when multiple subjects are permissible due to a relational (idafa) investigation.

Core Idea
A single science cannot have multiple subjects unless the investigation concerns a relation between two things.

This passage from commentaries on al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih tackles a foundational question: can a science like usul al-fiqh have more than one subject? The author rejects the common claim that medicine has two subjects (body and drugs), and instead provides a nuanced rule: if the science investigates a relation (idafa) between two things, and attributes from both relata are studied, then both can be subjects. Otherwise, unity of subject is necessary for unity of science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and counterexample
Recognize the common claim that a science can have multiple subjects (e.g., medicine with body and drugs). Note why the author challenges this claim.
The claim is widespread but the author declares it incorrect.
2
Recognize the key distinction
Understand the exception: if the science investigates a relation (idafa) between two things, then both relata can be subjects (e.g., usul al-fiqh with proofs and rulings).
The relation is central to accepting multiplicity.
3
Apply the reasoning to an example
See how the author refutes the medicine example: drugs are studied only for their effect on the body, so the subject remains the body alone.
The attributes of drugs are relevant only in relation to the body.
4
Synthesize the Hanafi principle
Conclude that unity of science depends on unity of its objects (masa'il), which derive from unity of subject. Multiplicity is only allowed when the investigated matter is a relation.
This is the mufta bihi position in Hanafi usul.

میں صحیح نہیں وہاں اثبات العلم سے مراد اثبات غلبة الظن ہو جائے گا جو مجازی معنی ہے تو جمع بین الحقیقة والمجاز لازم آئے گی جو صیح نہیں ہے لہذا ضروری ہے کہ عموم المجاز مراد لیا جائے یعنی الاعتقاد جازماً ساتھ متعلق تو نہیں لیکن چونکہ محصیل اور طالب علم ان مباحث سے مستغنی نہیں ہو سکتا اس لئے یہ مباحث بغرض افادہ پیش فرما دیئے۔ منها انهم قد ذكر و ان العلم الواحد الم یعنی علماء نے ذکر کیا ہے اور ان میں یہ امر شہرہ یافتہ ہے کہ قد يكون له اكثر من موضوع واحد یعنی ایک علم کے موضوع ایک سے زیادہ ہو سکتے ہیں یعنی علم ایک ہو اور موضوع اس کے متعدد ہوں یہ صحیح ہے کا لطب مثلاً جیسے علم الطب ایک علم ہے لیکن موضوع اس کے متعدد ہیں کیونکہ اس علم کے اندر بدنِ انسان کے احوال سے بحث کی جاتی ہے اور اسی طرح احوال ادویہ وغیرہ سے بھی بحث کی جاتی ہے تو اس فن کے دو موضوع ہیں ایک بدن انسان دوسرا ادویہ اس لئے ان دونوں کے احوال زیر بحث آتے ہیں مصنف نے اس کے متعلق فرمايا هذا غير صحیح کہ یہ بات تعدد موضوع والی صحیح نہیں۔ والتحقیق یعنی اس مسئلہ میں تحقیق حقیق یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی علم کے اندر اضافہ شئی الی آخر سے بحث کی جائے جیسے اصول فقہ میں کہ اس کے اندر اثبات الادلة للحکم یعنی اولہ کا حکم کو ثابت کرنا یہ زیر بحث آتا ہے اور جیسے منطق میں کہ ایصال تصور الى تصور وايصال تصديق إلى تصديق سے بحث کی جاتی ہے تو اس صورت میں وہ اور ایک کو دوسرے کی طرف راجع کرنے میں
For hukm, what is meant is establishing preponderance of opinion with the ruling. Therefore it is necessary that in establishing knowledge of the ruling, by knowledge is not meant solely firm belief, which is the real meaning of knowledge, because this is correct for the first three [sources] but not for analogy; there, establishing knowledge means establishing preponderance of opinion, which is a metaphorical meaning. Then combining the literal and metaphorical would be necessary, which is not correct. Therefore it is necessary to take the general metaphorical meaning, i.e., belief whether firm or not, and this general metaphor is correct for all proofs. The author said in al-Tawdih: 'Know that when I fell into the discussions of the subject...' The author presented three discussions regarding the subject and issues. Although they are not directly related to this discipline, since the student cannot do without them, he presented them for benefit. Among them: They have mentioned that a single science... meaning scholars have mentioned and it is well-known among them that a single science may have more than one subject. That is, a science is one and its subjects are multiple. This is correct, like medicine: the science of medicine is one science but its subjects are multiple, because in this science the conditions of the human body are discussed and also conditions of medicines, so this art has two subjects: one the human body and the second medicines. Therefore the conditions of both are discussed. The author said: 'This is not correct' that the multiplicity of subjects is correct. And the investigation: The truth in this matter is that if in a science the relation of one thing to another is discussed, like usul al-fiqh where establishing the proof for the ruling is discussed, and like logic where reaching a concept from a concept and reaching an assent from an assent is discussed, then the accidents that are related to the subject of investigation, some of them arise from one of the two relata and some from the other, like in usul al-fiqh some accidents arise from the proofs and some from the ruling, and in logic some from the means and some from the end. In that case both relata are subjects. The subject of usul al-fiqh is both proofs and rulings. And if the subject of investigation is not a relationship, then such a single science cannot have multiple objects. Because the unity of a science... this is the proof that a single science cannot have multiple subjects. The summary is that the unity and difference of a science depend on the unity and difference of its objects, and by objects are meant the issues. And difference of subject causes the objects and issues to differ. Therefore difference of subject necessarily causes difference of science. Hence a single science cannot have different subjects. And if you mean that our convention is to call such a science one, even if there is no meaning of unity, then such a convention is not valid nor can it be accepted. Moreover, then every person can set a convention that the science of fiqh and the science of geometry are one science with two subjects: one the act of the obligated person and the other measurement. Just as this convention is invalid, similarly your convention is not valid. And as for their example of medicine: that it is one science with two subjects (human body and medicines), the answer is that the subject of medicine is only the human body, and we discuss medicines only from the aspect that some medicines heal the body and some make it ill, so in reality this is also a discussion of the accidents and conditions of the human body. So the subject remains only the human body, and all discussions of medicine are related to its conditions. The commentator said in al-Talwih: 'Know that these three...' regarding the subject, the author opposed the verifiers, and whoever is aware of the discourse of the people in this place will be astonished at the author. First: the general statement that multiplicity of subjects for one art is permissible, even if more than two, but this is not absolutely correct. Rather the investigation: in a science the investigated thing either has a relationship between two things or not. In the first case, if the relationship is between two things, then it is considered. The accidents that intervene in the investigated thing: some arise from one relatum and some from the other, or not. In the first case, when both relata give rise to accidents, then both relata are subjects of that science, because ignoring one would be a preference without preponderator, and referring one to the other...
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage from a commentary on al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih discusses the foundational usul principle: can a single science (like usul al-fiqh) have more than one subject? The author begins by clarifying that 'establishing knowledge of the ruling' in the context of qiyas (analogy) means establishing preponderant opinion, not certain knowledge. He then introduces three discussions (mabahith) about the subject (mawdu') of a science. The first discussion: the widespread claim that a science can have multiple subjects. The author (of al-Tawdih) declares this incorrect. The investigation (tahqiq) distinguishes: if the science investigates a relation (idafa) between two things, then both relata can be subjects, as in usul al-fiqh (proofs and rulings) or logic (means and end). However, if there is no such relation, a single science cannot have multiple subjects. The proof: the unity of a science depends on the unity of its objects (masa'il), which depend on the subject. Different subjects necessarily produce different sciences. The passage rejects the idea of defining a science by arbitrary convention, using the absurd example of calling fiqh and geometry one science. It then refutes the example of medicine by arguing that medicine's true subject is the human body; medicines are discussed only relative to the body. The commentator (in al-Talwih) notes that the author's position opposes the verifiers (muhaqqiqun), causing astonishment. He further clarifies that the absolute claim of permissible multiplicity is incorrect; only when the investigated matter involves a relation between two things, and when attributes arise from each relatum, can both be subjects. The passage underscores the Hanafi usul method of rigorous definition of the subject of each science, ensuring systematic derivation of rulings.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawdu'): The subject matter or topic of a science, upon which its investigations are based.
اضافه (idafa): A relation or addition between two things, such as the relation of a proof to a ruling.
اتحاد العلم (ittihad al-'ilm): The unity of a science, determined by the unity of its subject and the objects (masa'il) derived from it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when can a single science have more than one subject?
A) When the science is subdivided into independent branches.
B) When the investigated matter involves a relation (idafa) between two things, and attributes arise from both relata.
C) When the scholar declares it by convention.
D) When the science is practical rather than theoretical.

Key Takeaway
The unity of a science depends on the unity of its subject; multiple subjects are allowed only when the science investigates a relation between them.

Method of Learning
intermediate
12 min
Unity of a Science's Subject-Matter: Proportion as Unifier
Analyzing conditions for multiple subjects in one science

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a single science requires its subjects to share a common proportion (tanasub), and distinguish between essential and accidental unity.

Core Idea
A science is one if its subjects share a proportion—either in essence or in goal—otherwise multiplicity dissolves unity.

How do we decide if a body of knowledge is a single science? This lesson tackles the foundational principle that a science's subject-matter must be unified. The Hanafi tradition argues that multiple subjects can belong to one science only if they share a meaningful proportion—an essential attribute (like quantity in geometry) or an accidental one (like health in medicine). This prevents arbitrary classifications and ensures coherence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
State the opening proposition: 'One science cannot have different subjects' and note the immediate rejection of conventionalist views.
This is the thesis the passage defends.
2
Analyze the first investigation: relation vs. non-relation
Examine the three scenarios: (1) the discussed object (mabḥūth ʿanhu) is a relation between two things, (2) it is not a relation, or (3) it is a relation but accidents from only one relatum matter. In case (1) where both relata contribute, both are subjects; otherwise only one.
Example: usul al-fiqh studies both proofs and rulings because they are relationally linked.
3
Examine the second investigation: critique of 'difference of masail'
Understand the critique: multiplicity of issues (kathrat al-masāʾil) does not cause science to differ; it is the lack of proportion (ʿadam al-tanāsub) among issues that does. Mere multiplicity of subjects doesn't guarantee proportion.
This refines the earlier claim.
4
Apply the third investigation: proportion as the unifying factor
Learn that multiple subjects are unified only if they share a common attribute—either essential (dhati) like quantity in geometry, or accidental ('aradi) like health in medicine. This is the relied-upon position.
The 'common accidental attribute' is typically the goal (gharah) of the science.

اور جب کسی علم میں محبوث عنہ اضافہ نہ ہو جیسے فقہ کہ فعل مکلف کے جوب و حرمتہ وغیرہ سے بحث کرتی ہے یا مبحوث عنہ اضافہ تو ہے لیکن احوال ناشیہ عن احد المضافین کو مبحوث عنہ میں کوئی دخل نہیں ہے جیسے منطق اختلاف مسائل کا موجب ہوتا ہے اور اختلاف مسائل موجب ہوتا ہے اختلاف علم کا کیونکہ یہ بدیہی امر ہے کہ اختلاف معلومات کے ساتھے علم مختلف ہو جاتا ہے اور معلومات مسائل علم کو کہتے ہیں ۔ فیه نظر حاصل نظریہ ہے کہ اختلا مسائل سے مراد اگر صرف تکثر مسائل ہے تو فلا نسلم انہ ہو جب ہم تسلیم نہیں کرتے کہ اختلاف بمعنی تکثر المسائل اختلاف علم کا موجب ہے۔ اور یہ امر بالکل ظاہر ہے کہ علم واحد کے مسائل تو کثیرہ ہوا کرتے ہیں اور اس کے باوجود علم واحد ہوتا ہے ۔ وان ارید عدم تناسبھا اور اگر اختلاف مسائل سے مراد ان کا عدم تناسب ہے یعنی مسائل ایسے مختلف ہوں کہ ان کے مابین تناسب نہ ہو تو اس قسم کا اختلاف مسائل اختلاف علوم کا موجب ہوتا ہے لیکن فلا تسلم ان مجرد تكثر الموضوعات الخ یہ تسلیم نہیں کرتے کہ صرف تکثر موضوعات اس قسم کے اختلاف کا موجب ہے وانما یلزم ذلک یعنی اس قسم کا اختلاف اس وقت لازم آتا ہے جب کہ موضوعا کثیرہ آپسمیں ایک دوسرے کے ساتھ مناسبت نہ رکھتے ہوں والقوم صرحوا الخ حالانکہ قوم اہل علم نے تصریح کی ہے کہ اشیاء کثیرہ اس وقت علم واحد کا موضوع بن سکتی ہیں جب ان میں تناسب موجود ہو درجہ التناسب اور وجہ تناسب کی تشریح یہ ہے کہ اشتراکھائی ذاتی یعنی وہ موضوعات متعددہ کسی امر زاتی میں مشترک ہوں جیسے علم الھندسہ کے موضوع خط- سطح - جسم تعلیمی ہیں یہ امور متعدد ہو کر المقدار یعنی کم متصل قار الذات میں اشتراک رکھتے ہیں جو ان کے لئے جنس ہے اور جنس امردائی ہوتا ہے یا کسی مرضی میں اشتراک رکھتے ہوں کبدن الانسان اور اس کے اجزاء اور غذائیں و ادویہ اور اس کے ارکان یعنی اخلاط اربعہ اور مزاج وغیرہ یہ سب موضوعات طب ہیں اور یہ صرف علم طب کی غایت یعنی صحت و تندرستی کے
This is that the unity and difference of a science depends on the unity and difference of its known objects (ma'lumat), and by known objects are meant the masail (issues). And due to difference of subject-matter, the known objects and masail become different. Therefore, based on difference of subject-matter, difference of sciences is necessary and unavoidable. Hence, one science cannot have different subjects.
And if you mean that it is our convention that we consider a science as one even if there is no meaning causing its unity, then such a convention is not valid and cannot be recognized. Otherwise, every person would be correct to establish a convention that the science of fiqh and the science of geometry are one science, and their subjects are two: one is the action of a responsible person, the other is quantity. So just as this convention is invalid, likewise your convention is also invalid. As for their example of the science of medicine that it is one science and its subjects are two: human body and drugs, the answer is that the subject of medicine is only the human body. As for drugs, we discuss them only in terms of how some drugs make the body healthy and some make it sick. So in reality, this is also a discussion of the accidents and states of the human body. Thus, the subject remains only the human body, and all discussions of medicine pertain to its states. The commentator in al-Talwih said: 'Know that these three...' regarding the subject, in these three investigations the author opposed the researchers, and one who knows the discourse of the people will wonder at the author.
First: That which is commonly said that for one art multiple subjects are permissible, even if more than two, is not absolutely correct. Rather, the investigation is that the discussed object (mabahith 'anh) in a science is either a relation between two things or not. On the first case, if the relation is between two things, then we look: if the accidents involved in the discussed object are some arising from one of the two relata and some from the other, then both relata are subjects for that science, because ignoring one is a preference without preponderance, and reducing one to the other is forced. For example, in usul al-fiqh, the discussion is about the establishment of proofs from rulings. Now the states involved in the discussed object include some arising from the proof (e.g., generality, commonality, tawatur) and some arising from the ruling (e.g., being worship or punishment). So the subject of this art is both proofs and rulings. As for when the discussed object is not a relation, like fiqh which discusses obligation, prohibition, etc. of the act of a responsible person, or the discussed object is a relation but the accidents arising from one of the relata have no involvement in the discussed object, like logic which discusses the conveyance to concept and assent (tasdiq) but the states of the conveyed concept and assent are not involved, then in these cases the subject of the science can only be one, not multiple. This is because difference of subject causes difference of masail, and difference of masail causes difference of sciences, as it is self-evident that with difference of known objects, science differs, and known objects are called the masail of a science.
There is a critique: The conclusion is that if by 'difference of masail' is meant merely multiplicity of masail, then we do not accept that it causes difference of sciences. It is obvious that one science can have many masail and still be one science. If by 'difference of masail' is meant their lack of proportion, i.e., the masail are so diverse that there is no proportion between them, then this kind of difference does cause difference of sciences. But we do not accept that mere multiplicity of subjects causes this kind of difference. Rather, this kind of difference only occurs when multiple subjects have no proportion with each other. And the scholars have explicitly stated that multiple things can be the subject of one science only when there is proportion among them. The rank of proportion and its explanation is that they share a common essential attribute (amr dhati), like the subject of geometry: line, surface, and solid (volume) which share the attribute of quantity (al-miqdar), which is their genus and is an essential matter. Or they share a common accidental attribute (amr 'aradi), like the human body and its parts, foods, drugs, and its elements (i.e., four humors, temperament, etc.) — all these are subjects of medicine, and they only come together because of the goal of medicine, which is health and wellness.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the principle that a single science must have a unified subject-matter. The discussion begins by stating the common view that difference in subjects necessitates difference in sciences, so one science cannot have multiple subjects. The opponent argues that subject-matter is a matter of convention, but the author rejects this as arbitrary. Using the example of medicine, the author demonstrates that what appears to be multiple subjects actually reduces to one: the human body, since all discussions of drugs relate to their effect on the body.

The commentator then presents three investigations regarding the subject of a science. The first investigation argues that the claim that multiple subjects are permissible for one art is not absolute. If the discussed object involves a relation between two things, and the accidents discussed arise from both relata equally, then both are subjects (e.g., usul al-fiqh deals with both proofs and rulings). If the relation is not involved, or only one relatum contributes the discussed accidents, the subject is singular.

The second investigation raises a critique: if 'difference of masail' means mere multiplicity, it does not cause sciences to differ, because one science can have many questions. Only when masail lack proportion does difference occur. Mere multiplicity of subjects does not necessarily cause lack of proportion; it depends on whether the subjects share a common attribute.

The third investigation clarifies that multiple subjects can constitute one science only if they share a proportion, either in a common essential attribute (like quantity in geometry) or an accidental attribute (like health in medicine). This is the relied-upon position of the scholars.

The passage is a rigorous usul analysis, typical of Hanafi scholarship, emphasizing that the unity of a science is not arbitrary but based on objective features of the subject matter.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawḍūʿ): The subject-matter of a science; that which the science investigates in terms of its accidental properties (ʿawāriḍ).
علم (ʿilm): A discipline or body of knowledge consisting of masāʾil (issues) that share a common subject and proportion.
مبحوث عنہ (mabḥūth ʿanhu): That which is investigated in a science; the object of inquiry, which may be a single thing or a relation between two things.
اضافہ (iḍāfah): A relation or addition between two things; the state of being relative.
تناسب (tanāsub): Proportion or correlation between multiple subjects, which is necessary for them to belong to one science.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when can a single science have multiple subjects?
A) When they are conventionally agreed upon by scholars.
B) When the subjects share a common attribute (essential or accidental) that creates a proportion between them.
C) When the subjects are all derived from the same primary text.
D) When the science deals with both proofs and rulings.

Key Takeaway
Multiple subjects can form one science only when they share a proportion (tanāsub)—either an essential common attribute or a related accidental attribute like the discipline's goal.

Method of Learning
intermediate
8 min
Subject of a Science: Multiplicity and Unity in Hanafi Usul
Analyzing conditions for multiple subjects under one science

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a science can have multiple subjects if they are proportionally related (تناسب), and evaluate the opposing claim that a science must have a single subject.

Core Idea
A science can have multiple subjects if they share an essential or accidental proportionality.

In this passage from a scholarly debate, the Hanafi position on the subject matter of a science is defended. The author argues that multiplicity of subjects does not necessitate multiple sciences, as long as there is a unifying link—either essential (like genus) or accidental (like common goal). This principle has direct implications for usul al-fiqh, whose scope includes multiple proofs and rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central dispute
Determine the question being debated: can a single science have more than one subject matter? Identify the two opposing positions.
The dispute is about the condition for unity of a science, not about the definition of science itself.
2
State the Hanafi position and its condition
Articulate the Hanafi view: multiple subjects are allowed if they are proportionally related (تناسب). Distinguish between essential (ذاتی) and accidental (عرضی) proportionality.
Essential proportionality is sharing a genus (e.g., quantity for geometry); accidental is sharing a goal (e.g., health for medicine).
3
Identify the opposing argument
The opponent (commentator) argues that difference in subjects necessarily leads to difference in sciences, implying a science must have one subject.
The opponent's proof is based on the self-evident principle that different known things produce different sciences.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal: mere multiplicity does not cause difference; only lack of proportionality does. The opponent's examples (like fiqh and geometry) fail because they lack any proportionality.
The author cites scholarly consensus ('القوم صرحوا') that proportionality is the key.
5
Apply to usul al-fiqh
Using the principle, conclude that usul al-fiqh can have multiple subjects (Book, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas) because they share the common goal of establishing rulings.
This broadens the scope of usul compared to a view that restricts it to a single subject.

تکلف ہے جیسے اصول فقہ میں بحث ہوتی ہے اثبات الادلۃ الاحکام سے اب وہ احوال جن کو مبحوث عنہ میں دخل ہے بعض ناشی عن الدلیل ہیں جیسے دلیل کا عام ہونا یا مشترک ہونا اور متواتر ہونا اور بعض احوال ایسے ہیں جو ناشی عن الحكم میں لكونه عبادہ اور عقوبة تو اس فن کا موضوع ادلہ اور احکام دونوں ہوں گے واما اذا لم یکن اور جب کسی علم میں محبوث عنہ اضافہ نہ ہو جیسے فقہ کہ فعل مکلف کے جوب و حرمتہ وغیرہ سے بحث کرتی ہے یا مبحوث عنہ اضافہ تو ہے لیکن احوال ناشیہ عن احد المضافین کو مبحوث عنہ میں کوئی دخل نہیں ہے جیسے منطق کہ تصور و تصدیق کے ایصال الى التصور والتصدیق سے بحث کرتی ہے لیکن تصور و تصدیق موصل الیہ کے احوال کو دخل نہیں ہے تو ان صورتوں میں موضوع علم صرف ایک ہو سکتا ہے متعدد نہیں ہو سکتے کیونکہ اختلاف موضوع اختلاف مسائل کا موجب ہوتا ہے اور اختلاف مسائل موجب ہوتا ہے اختلاف علم کا کیونکہ یہ بدیہی امر ہے کہ اختلاف معلومات کے ساتھے علم مختلف ہو جاتا ہے اور معلومات مسائل علم کو کہتے ہیں ۔ فیه نظر حاصل نظریہ ہے کہ اختلا مسائل سے مراد اگر صرف تکثر مسائل ہے تو فلا نسلم انہ ہو جب ہم تسلیم نہیں کرتے کہ اختلاف بمعنی تکثر المسائل اختلاف علم کا موجب ہے۔ اور یہ امر بالکل ظاہر ہے کہ علم واحد کے مسائل تو کثیرہ ہوا کرتے ہیں اور اس کے باوجود علم واحد ہوتا ہے ۔ وان ارید عدم تناسبھا اور اگر اختلاف مسائل سے مراد ان کا عدم تناسب ہے یعنی مسائل ایسے مختلف ہوں کہ ان کے مابین تناسب نہ ہو تو اس قسم کا اختلاف مسائل اختلاف علوم کا موجب ہوتا ہے لیکن فلا تسلم ان مجرد تكثر الموضوعات الخ یہ تسلیم نہیں کرتے کہ صرف تکثر موضوعات اس قسم کے اختلاف کا موجب ہے وانما یلزم ذلک یعنی اس قسم کا اختلاف اس وقت لازم آتا ہے جب کہ موضوعا کثیرہ آپسمیں ایک دوسرے کے ساتھ مناسبت نہ رکھتے ہوں والقوم صرحوا الخ حالانکہ قوم اہل علم نے تصریح کی ہے کہ اشیاء کثیرہ اس وقت علم واحد کا موضوع بن سکتی ہیں جب ان میں تناسب موجود ہو درجہ التناسب اور وجہ تناسب کی تشریح یہ ہے کہ اشتراکھائی ذاتی یعنی وہ موضوعات متعددہ کسی امر زاتی میں مشترک ہوں جیسے علم الھندسہ کے موضوع خط- سطح - جسم تعلیمی ہیں یہ امور متعدد ہو کر المقدار یعنی کم متصل قار الذات میں اشتراک رکھتے ہیں جو ان کے لئے جنس ہے اور جنس امردائی ہوتا ہے یا کسی مرضی میں اشتراک رکھتے ہوں کبدن الانسان اور اس کے اجزاء اور غذائیں و ادویہ اور اس کے ارکان یعنی اخلاط اربعہ اور مزاج وغیرہ یہ سب موضوعات طب ہیں اور یہ صرف علم طب کی غایت یعنی صحت و تندرستی کے تحصیل میں مشارک میں تو صحت ایک امر عرضی ہے جس میں انھیں اشتراک ہے تو سے معلوم ہوا کہ قوم نے موضوعات متعددہ میں رعایت تناسب کو نظر انداز نہیں کیا جو وحدہ علم کی موجب ہوتی ہے ۔ وان ليس لاحد اور یہ بالکل جائز نہیں ہے کہ کوئی ایک اصطلاح کرلے کہ فقہ اور ہندسہ علم واحد ہے لیکن موضوع اس کے دو ہیں ایک فعل مکلف دوسرا مقدار جس کا مصنف نے الزام دیا ہے جب کہ ان کے اندر کوئی تناسب موجود نہیں ہے تو یہ علم واحد نہیں ہو سکتے لهذا الزام مصنف صحیح نہیں ہے ۔ ثمانه فیما اور رات یہ بھی مصنف پر اعتراض ہے کہ مصنف نے جو مثالیں پیش کی ہیں ان میں خود اپنے آپ کے ساتھ مناقض ہے کیونکہ اصول فقہ کا موضوع اشیاء کثیرہ ہیں کیونکہ اصول فقہ کا موضوع صرف مفہوم دلیل تو نہیں ہے کیونکہ محمولات مسائل مفهوم دلیل کے اعراض ذاتیہ نہیں بلکہ یہ کتاب - سنت ۔ اجماع - قیاس کے اعراض ذاتیہ ہیں بعض على الانفراد بعض على التشارک تو اس کا موضوع امور اربعہ ہو گئے اور اسی طرح تصور اور تصدیق جو موضوع منطق ہیں یہ بھی امور متعددہ ہیں۔ شارح نے مصنف پر حسب عادت جو اعتراض کئے ہیں ان کی حقیقت کچھ نہیں شارح نے جو یہ کہا ہے کہ اختلاف مسائل سے کیا مراد ہے تکثر مسائل یا عدم تناسب فیما بین المسائل تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ صرف تکثر مسائل مراد نہیں ہے بلکہ عدم تناسب مراد ہے اصل بات یہ ہے کہ حقیقت علم مسائل ہوا کرتے ہیں اگر مسائل آپسمیں غیر متنا سب ہیں تو یہ ایک ایسا اختلاف ہے جو اختلاف علم کا موجب ہے اور جب مسائل ہی حقیقت علم ہوئے تو مسائل دو اجزا پر مشتمل ہوتے ہیں ایک موضوع مسئلہ دوسرا محمول اور موضوع مسئلہ کا رجوع موضوع علم کی طرف ہوتا ہے جس کی تفصیل گزر چکی ہے کہ موضوع مسلہ یا موضوع علم ہوگا یا اس کا نوع وغیرہ اور محمول مسئلہ کا رجوع موضوع فن کے اعراض ذاتیہ کی طرف ہوتا ہے اگر مسائل کے مابین ان دونوں اجزا میں تناسب ہو تو ایسے مسائل کے مابین اتحاد ہوا جو اتحاد علم کا مورب ہے اگر ان دونوں جزئین میں سے کسی ایک میں اختلاف ہو خواہ اختلاف في الموضوع ہو یا في المحمول تو یہ مسائل مختلف قرار پائیں گے اور مسائل کا ایسا اختلاف یہ موجب ہوتا ہے۔ اختلاف علم کا تو جس وقت محمولات مسائل راجع ہوں اضافہ مخصوصہ کی طرف کہ بعض ناشی عن احد المضافین ہوں اور بعض ناشی عن الآخر تو اس وقت موضوع متعدد ہوگا لیکن علم واحد قرار پائیگا کیونکہ کسی ایک مضاف کے حالات کو نظر انداز کرنا ترجیح بلا مرجع ہے اور ایک سے
It is a difficulty, as in usul al-fiqh, investigation is about the proofs of rulings. Now those states that are involved in the subject-matter of investigation are some arising from the proof, like the proof being general or common or mutawatir, and some states are those arising from the ruling, like being worship or punishment. So the subject of this art would be both proofs and rulings. And as for when there is no addition in a science, like fiqh which discusses the obligation and prohibition etc. of the act of the responsible person, or there is addition but the states arising from one of the two added things have no involvement in the subject-matter, like logic which discusses the delivery of concept and verification to the concept and verification, but the states of the concept and verification that are delivered are not involved, then in these cases the subject of the science can only be one, not multiple, because difference of subject causes difference of problems, and difference of problems causes difference of sciences, because it is self-evident that with difference of known things the science becomes different, and the known things are called the problems of the science. There is a consideration: the gained theory is that if by difference of problems is meant mere multiplicity of problems, then we do not accept that it causes difference of science. And this is entirely clear that the problems of a single science are many, yet it is a single science. And if by difference of problems is meant their lack of proportionality, i.e., the problems are so different that there is no proportionality between them, then this kind of difference of problems causes difference of sciences. But we do not accept that mere multiplicity of subjects causes this kind of difference. Rather, this kind of difference occurs only when multiple subjects have no relation with each other. And the people (scholars) have stated that multiple things can become the subject of a single science only when there is proportionality among them. The degree of proportionality and the explanation of the cause of proportionality is that they share in an essential matter, i.e., the multiple subjects share in some essential thing, like the subjects of geometry — line, surface, and solid body — these multiple matters share in quantity, i.e., continuous and fixed-in-essence quantity, which is their genus, and genus is an essential thing. Or they share in an accidental matter, like the human body and its parts, and foods and medicines, and its elements — i.e., the four humors and temperament etc. — all these are subjects of medicine, and they only share in the goal of medicine, i.e., achieving health. Health is an accidental matter in which they share. So it is known that the people did not disregard the observation of proportionality in multiple subjects, which causes the unity of a science. And it is not permissible for anyone to make a terminology that fiqh and geometry are a single science, but its subject is two: one is the act of the responsible person, the other is quantity, which the author has objected to. But when there is no proportionality between them, it cannot be a single science. Hence the objection of the author is not correct. And there is also an objection against the author that the examples he presented are self-contradictory, because the subject of usul al-fiqh is multiple things, because the subject of usul al-fiqh is not merely the concept of the proof, because the predicates of the problems are not essential accidents of the concept of the proof, rather they are essential accidents of the Book, Sunnah, consensus, and qiyas — some individually, some collectively — so its subject becomes the four things. Similarly, concept and verification, which are the subjects of logic, are also multiple matters. The commentator has raised objections against the author as usual; their reality is nothing. The commentator said: what is meant by difference of problems — multiplicity or lack of proportionality between them? The answer is that mere multiplicity is not meant, rather lack of proportionality is meant. The real matter is that the reality of science is the problems. If the problems are disproportionate among themselves, then this is a difference that causes difference of sciences. And when the problems are the reality of science, then problems consist of two parts: one is the subject of the problem, the other is the predicate. And the subject of the problem returns to the subject of the science, the detail of which has passed: that the subject of the problem is either the subject of the science or its type etc. And the predicate of the problem returns to the essential accidents of the subject of the art. If there is proportionality between the problems in these two parts, then there is unity between such problems, which necessitates unity of science. If there is difference in either of these two parts, whether difference in the subject or in the predicate, then these problems will be considered different, and such difference of problems causes difference of sciences. So when the predicates of the problems return to a specific addition (i.e., one of the two added things), and some arise from one of the added things and some from the other, then the subject will be multiple but will be considered a single science, because ignoring the states of one of the added things would be a preference without a ground. And from one... (the text cuts off)
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of a usul al-fiqh principle regarding the definition of a science's subject matter. The author is defending the position that a science can have multiple subjects as long as there is a proper relation (تناسب) between them, such as sharing in an essential characteristic (جنس) or an accidental goal (غرض). The author refutes the claim that mere multiplicity of subjects necessitates multiple sciences, using examples from usul al-fiqh, fiqh, geometry, logic, and medicine. The passage also addresses objections raised by the commentator (شارح) against the author (مصنف), clarifying that the disagreement is about the interpretation of 'difference of problems'. The author argues that the unity of a science depends on the proportionality of its problems both in subject and predicate. The text follows a standard scholarly debate structure: statement of principle, objection, and response.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawḍūʿ): The subject matter of a science; that which the science studies in its problems.
تناسب (tanāsub): Proportionality or appropriate relation between multiple subjects, allowing them to be studied under one science.
مسائل (masāʾil): The problems or issues that constitute the reality of a science; each consists of a subject and a predicate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented in this passage, under what condition can a science have multiple subjects?
A) When the subjects are completely different in nature.
B) When the subjects share an essential or accidental proportionality (تناسب).
C) When the subjects are all essential accidents of the same predicate.
D) When the subjects are only two in number.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position allows multiple subjects for a science if they are proportionally related, which is key to defining the scope of usul al-fiqh.

Method of Learning
intermediate
10 min
Unity of a Science's Subject Despite Multiple Topics
Refuting objections that topic multiplicity forces science multiplicity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a single science has one subject, identifying its species and extensions as accidents, and refute the objection that multiple topics imply multiple sciences.

Core Idea
A science's unity is preserved by a single subject; its diverse topics are species or extensions, not separate subjects.

This passage tackles a foundational methodological question in Hanafi usul: Can a single science legitimately discuss multiple, apparently unrelated topics? The author argues that as long as those topics are related as species or essential accidents of one core subject, the science remains unified. The commentator's objections are addressed through concrete examples from geometry, grammar, and usul al-fiqh itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested claim
Recognize the commentator's argument: that subjects like Book, Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas in usul al-fiqh are multiple, so the science has multiple subjects.
The objection assumes that each distinct topic must have its own subject.
2
Locate the Hanafi principle
Understand the author's rule: a science investigates the essential accidents (a'radh dhatiyya) of one subject. Its various topics are either species of that subject or accidents that belong to the subject through those species.
Example: geometry's subject is magnitude; lines and surfaces are its species.
3
Apply the principle to the objection
Show that in usul al-fiqh, the single subject is 'shar'i proof' (dalil shar'i). The four sources (Book, Sunnah, etc.) are extensions (masadiq) of this subject. Their accidents are discussed as accidents of the subject, not of independent subjects.
Thus, discussing Ijma's authority is discussing an accident of shar'i proof, not a separate subject.
4
Refute the 'shared accident' argument
The commentator claims that sharing in an accident (like 'being an accident' or 'aiming at health') could unify sciences, but this leads to absurdities (e.g., fiqh and geometry would be one science). The author shows that a proper unifying relation must be essential, not accidental.
A genuine proportion (tanasub) is needed, such as a single relation (idafa) or genus-species hierarchy.
5
Consolidate with author's conclusion
Accept the author's verification: 'the truth is most worthy of being followed'. The principle ensures proper classification of Islamic sciences without artificial multiplicity.
This principle underlies how Hanafi usul organizes its subject matter.

It is difficult to twist and turn circumstances and refer them to another; therefore these two mudaf (added things) will be the subject of the art, yet the knowledge and art will be one because when the relation (idafa) is under discussion, the mentioned proportion exists in all those issues. Because at that time the single relation between the two subjects will become a unifier, and similarly there will be proportion in the predicates because of the states of the objects of one relation. And when the relation is not under discussion, then that proportion cannot be established; in such a situation, the subject of one science cannot be multiple, rather multiplicity of subjects will lead to multiplicity of sciences. After this, the commentator, while presenting the reason for proportion, said 'shared in the essential with consideration of [؟]', this is also a fallacy because at that time that very essential which is common among the species will be the subject of the art, not the multiple species. Like the science of geometry: its subject is continuous quantity (al-kamm al-muttasil al-qar al-dhat) i.e., magnitude; lines, surfaces, and teaching bodies etc. are its species, not the subject itself. And in the art, the essential accidents of the subject are discussed; similarly, the essential accidents of the species of the subject are also discussed. These are in reality accidents of the subject of the art through the mediation of the species. For example, the subject of grammar is 'kalima' (word), and the states of its species—noun, verb, particle—are discussed. From this it does not follow that the subject of grammar is noun, verb, particle; rather the subject of the art is 'kalima', and the accidents of these divisions are accidents of 'kalima'. In any case, the subject of geometry is magnitude (continuous quantity), which is a single thing. And what the commentator said further: 'a shared accident'—this proportion is also not a complete proportion on the basis of which multiple subjects can be the subject of a single science merely through sharing in an accident. Because what is meant by 'accidental'? If by it is meant absolute accident, then it would necessitate that fiqh and geometry be a single science, because the subject of fiqh is the act of the obligated person, and the subject of geometry is magnitude; both share being accidents—the act of the obligated person is not a substance but an accident, similarly magnitude as continuous quantity is an accident. If by accident is meant a specific kind of accident, like the commentator said about the science of medicine: the human body, foods, drugs—these share in health, which is the goal of the art. Then that too is insufficient; otherwise it would necessitate that all Arabic sciences—grammar, etymology, prosody—be a single science, because the subject of all is words, and the purpose and goal of all is avoidance of verbal error, yet they are not a single science. So it is clear that this kind of proportion is insufficient for the unity of science. Then after this, the commentator raised an objection of contradiction: that the author, in the examples he gave, contradicts himself because the subjects of usul al-fiqh are many things, since the predicates that are issues in usul al-fiqh are not accidents of the meaning of shar'i proof, but rather they are accidents of the Book, Sunnah, Ijma, etc. The commentator's objection is also invalid because whatever the subject of an art is, its accidents of meaning are not discussed; rather the accidents of its extension (misdāq) are discussed. For example, grammar does not discuss the accidents of the meaning of 'kalima', but its extension—noun, verb, particle—their accidents are discussed. From this it does not follow that all these things are called subjects. Similarly, the subject of usul al-fiqh is shar'i proof; its four extensions—Book, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas—discussing their accidents is discussing the accidents of shar'i proof, which is the subject of usul. Similarly, the subject of logic is that which leads to the unknown (mūṣil ilā al-majhūl), which is a single thing, then it is general whether it is conceptual (taṣawwurī) or assivative (taṣdīqī); in reality there is no multiplicity of subject, rather the subject is a single thing, though its species are many. In any case, the author's investigation is praiseworthy; the truth is most worthy of being followed.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of objections concerning the unity of a science's subject matter. The author argues that while a science may deal with multiple topics, they can be subsumed under a single subject if they share a unifying relation (iḍāfa) or are essential accidents of that subject. He uses examples from geometry, grammar, and logic to illustrate that diverse instances do not necessitate multiple sciences. The commentator's objections—that sharing in accidents alone is insufficient, and that uṣūl al-fiqh has multiple subjects—are shown to be fallacious. The key point is that the subject of an art is a single entity; its types and their accidents are discussed as accidents of that subject, not as separate subjects. This principle is fundamental to the organization of Islamic sciences.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawḍūʿ): The subject matter of a science; the single entity whose essential accidents are investigated.
عوارض ذاتیہ (aʿrāḍ dhātiyya): Essential accidents that necessarily follow from the subject's essence.
مصداق (miṣdāq): Extension; the concrete individual instances that fall under a concept. In usul, the four sources are masadiq of shar'i proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the subject of usul al-fiqh?
A) The Book, Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas
B) Shar'i proof (dalīl sharʿī)
C) The acts of the obligated person
D) The essential accidents of the law

Key Takeaway
A single science has one subject; its diverse topics are species or extensions of that subject, and discussing their accidental properties does not create multiple subjects.

The Two Meanings of Haythiyya in Subject Definition
Explaining the dual meaning of 'haythiyya' in subject definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two meanings of 'haythiyya' in the definition of a science's subject and explain how each meaning affects what is discussed in that science.

Core Idea
The 'haythiyya' either is part of the subject or describes the accidents studied; this decides the scope of the science.

The term 'haythiyya' (consideration) is crucial in defining the subject-matter of any science, especially in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. This lesson unpacks its dual meaning as explained in Al-Tawdih. Understanding this distinction helps you delineate what a science investigates and what it takes for granted.

Guided Path
1
Recall the context
This passage discusses the second section concerning haythiyya in the subject of a science. It states that haythiyya has two meanings.
2
Identify the first meaning
The first meaning: haythiyya is a part of the subject itself. For example, in 'the existent qua existent' as the subject of theology, 'qua existent' is the haythiyya and is a component of the subject.
In this case, essential accidents that follow from that aspect are discussed, but the haythiyya itself is not investigated within the science.
3
Identify the second meaning
The second meaning: haythiyya describes the essential accidents under discussion of the subject. The haythiyya indicates which accidents are studied.
The passage mentions this as the description of the essential accidents.
4
Contrast the two meanings
In the first meaning, the haythiyya is included in the subject and limits which accidents are discussed. In the second, the haythiyya itself is the characterization of the accidents being investigated.
The subject and its parts are 'taken for granted' (مفروغ عنہا) in the science and studied elsewhere.
5
Apply the distinction
For any science, determine whether the haythiyya is an integral part of the subject or a description of the accidents. This clarifies the scope of investigation.
For example, in usul al-fiqh, the subject is 'the proofs of fiqh in general'; the haythiyya 'in general' is part of the subject.

قال المصنف في التوضيح ومنها انه قد تذكر الحيثية یہ مبحث ثانی ہے چونکہ موضوع فن کسی نہ کسی حیثیت کے ساتھ محبت ہوتا ہے تو یہ بحث اسی حیثیت کی تحقیق کے ساتھ متعلق ہے تو کہا لها معنیان یعنی حیثیت سے دو معانی مراد لئے جاسکتے ہیں ۔ احدهما ان الشئی الخر یعنی وہ شئی محبت اس حیثیت کے ساتھ مل کر موضوع فن ہوتا ہے یعنی حیثیت بھی موضوع کا ایک حصہ اور جز ہوتی ہے جیسے شئی محبت موضوع کا حصہ ہے كما يقال الموجود من حیث یعنی جیسے کہا جاتا ہے کہ موجود من حیث انہ موجود علم الہی کا موضوع ہے تو یہ حیثیت داخل في الموضوعية ہے تو اس صورت میں ان اعراض ذاتیہ سے بحث کی جائیگی جو موجود کو اسی حیثیت موجودیت سے لاحق ہوں گے جیسے وحدة وكثرة وغیرہ ہیں اور خود اسی حیثیت کو زیر بحث نہیں لایا جائیگا کیونکہ علم میں موضوع کے اعراض ذاتیہ سے بحث کی جاتی ہے خود ذات موضوع اور اس کے اجزا سے بحث نہیں کی جاتی مشہور مقولہ ہے کہ موضوع فن اور اس کے اجزا مفروغ عنہا فی ہذا الفن اور مبحوث عنہا فی فن آخر ہوتا ہے ۔ دثانیھما اور حیثیت کا دوسرا معنی یہ ہوتا ہے کہ حیثیت موضوع کے اعراض ذاتیہ مبحوثہ کا بیان ہوتی ہے کیونکہ ایک شئی کے اعراض ذاتیہ
The author said in Al-Tawdih: 'And among them is that haythiyya may be mentioned.' This is the second section. Since the subject of an art is loved with some haythiyya, this discussion relates to the investigation of that haythiyya. So he said: It has two meanings. The first: that the thing loved together with this haythiyya forms the subject of the art, i.e., haythiyya is also a part and component of the subject. For example, as it is said: 'the existent qua existent' is the subject of theology (ilāhī), so this haythiyya is included in subjecthood. In this case, the essential accidents that befall the existent in that aspect of existence are discussed, such as unity and multiplicity etc., and the haythiyya itself is not brought under discussion, because in a science the essential accidents of the subject are discussed, not the subject's essence itself or its parts. The well-known saying is that the subject of the art and its parts are taken for granted in this art and are discussed in another art. The second meaning: haythiyya is the description of the essential accidents under discussion of the subject, because the essential accidents of a thing...
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'haythiyya' (consideration) as it relates to the subject matter of a science. The author from Al-Tawdih explains that haythiyya has two meanings. First, it can be a component of the subject itself, such as 'existent qua existent' in theology: the aspect 'qua existent' is part of the subject. In this case, we discuss accidents that follow from that specific consideration, not the consideration itself. The subject and its parts are assumed within the science and not investigated further; they are studied in another science. Second, haythiyya can indicate the necessary accidents that are discussed about the subject. This distinction is important for correctly delineating the scope of a science.

Key Terms
حیثیت (haythiyya): A consideration or aspect under which a subject is studied; it either forms part of the subject or describes the accidents discussed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first meaning of 'haythiyya'?
A) It is the description of essential accidents
B) It is a part of the subject itself
C) It is the goal of the science
D) It is the predicate of the issue

Key Takeaway
The haythiyya in a science's subject definition has two possible meanings: as a part of the subject or as a description of the accidents to be discussed; this distinction determines the boundaries of investigation.

Two Meanings of Haythiyya in Defining the Subject of a Science
Explaining the two meanings of haythiyya in defining the subject of a science

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two meanings of haythiyya in the definition of a science and apply the correct meaning to describe the subject of a given discipline.

Core Idea
Haythiyya has two meanings: as part of the subject or as a pointer to the accidents studied; the latter is the operative meaning.

In defining the subject-matter of a science, scholars often add an 'aspect' (haythiyya) to specify the angle of investigation. This passage from the commentary on al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih clarifies that this aspect can be understood in two distinct ways. Understanding this distinction is crucial for properly delimiting the scope of any discipline, including usul al-fiqh. Let us unpack the two meanings and see why the second one is the correct usage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context of the discussion
The passage first refutes an objection that usul al-fiqh has multiple subjects, then introduces the two meanings of haythiyya. Recognize that this is a methodological clarification.
The objection is about the multiplicity of subjects, but the author unifies them under one subject: legal evidence.
2
Understand the first meaning
In the first meaning, the aspect is part of the subject itself. The essential accidents that attach to the thing under that aspect are studied, but the aspect itself is not discussed in that science.
Example: 'the existent qua existent' in divine science—the aspect 'qua existent' is included in the subject.
3
Understand the second meaning
In the second meaning, the aspect indicates which type of essential accidents are to be studied. Here, the aspect itself becomes the object of investigation.
Example: 'the human body in terms of health and illness' in medicine—health and illness are themselves studied.
4
Apply the distinction to examples
The passage uses medicine and astronomy to illustrate: in medicine, health and illness are discussed; in astronomy, shape is discussed. This shows the second meaning is correct.
If the first meaning were intended, these aspects would not be discussed, but they are.
5
Recognize the intended meaning
The commentator states plainly that the intended meaning is the second, as confirmed by the practice of actual sciences.
The phrase 'يراد به المعنى الثاني' (the intended meaning is the second) is explicit.

یہ مبحث ثانی ہے چونکہ موضوع فن کسی نہ کسی حیثیت کے ساتھ مہتم بہ ہوتا ہے تو یہ بحث اسی حیثیت کی تحقیق کے ساتھ متعلق ہے تو کہا لها معنیان یعنی حیثیت سے دو معانی مراد لئے جاسکتے ہیں ۔ احدهما ان الشی الخر یعنی وہ شے مہتم بہ اس حیثیت کے ساتھ مل کر موضوع فن ہوتا ہے یعنی حیثیت بھی موضوع کا ایک حصہ اور جز ہوتی ہے جیسے شے مہتم بہ موضوع کا حصہ ہے كما يقال الموجود من حیث یعنی جیسے کہا جاتا ہے کہ موجود من حیث انہ موجود علم الہی کا موضوع ہے تو یہ حیثیت داخل فی الموضوعیہ ہے تو اس صورت میں ان اعراض ذاتیہ سے بحث کی جائیگی جو موجود کو اسی حیثیت موجودیت سے لاحق ہوں گے جیسے وحدة وكثرة وغیرہ ہیں اور خود اسی حیثیت کو زیر بحث نہیں لایا جائیگا کیونکہ علم میں موضوع کے اعراض ذاتیہ سے بحث کی جاتی ہے خود ذات موضوع اور اس کے اجزا سے بحث نہیں کی جاتی مشہور مقولہ ہے کہ موضوع فن اور اس کے اجزا مفروغ عنہا فی ہذا الفن اور مبحوث عنہا فی فن آخر ہوتا ہے ۔ دثانیھما اور حیثیت کا دوسرا معنی یہ ہوتا ہے کہ حیثیت موضوع کے اعراض ذاتیہ مبحوثہ کا بیان ہوتی ہے کیونکہ ایک شئی کے اعراض ذاتیہ متنوعه یعنی مختلفتہ النوع ہوتے ہیں اور ایک علم میں موضوع کے ایک نوع اعراض کی بحث ہوتی ہے اور حیثیت اسی نوع مبحوثہ کا بیان ہوتی ہے جیسے کہ ان کا قول ہے کہ موضوع طب بدن انسان ہے من حيث انه يصلح وبمرض اور موضوع ہيئة اجسام العالم من حيث ان لها شكلا ہے تو یہ حیثیت ان اعراض ذاتیہ مبحوثہ عنہا کا بیان ہے ۔
يراد به المعنى الثاني یعنی انها بيان للاعراض المبحوثه لا الداخل یعنی یہ مراد نہیں ہے کہ یہ موضوع کی جز ہو کر داخل فی الموضوعیہ ہے یہی وجہ ہے کہ طب میں خود صحت اور مرض سے بحث کی جاتی ہے اور ہیئت میں شکل سے بحث کی جاتی ہے۔ فلو كان المراد هو الاول یعنی اگر یہ حیثیت جزء موضوع ہوتی تو پھر واجب تھا کہ طب وہیئت میں ان اعراض سے بحث کی جاتی جو ان حیثیتین مذکور تین کے واسطہ سے لاحق ہوتے اور خود ان حیثیتین سے بحث نہ کی جاتی والواقع خلاف ذلك یعنی نفس الامر میں ایسے نہیں ہے بلکہ خود ان حیثیتین سے بحث کی جاتی ہے۔ قال الشارح في التلويح قوله منها انه قد تذكر الحيثية

یہ مصنف کی جانب سے مبحث ثانی ہے جس میں اس حیثیت کی تحقیق ہے جو موضوع فن کے ساتھ مذکور ہوتی ہے مثلا یوں کہا جاتا ہے کہ اس علم کا موضوع مو ذالك الشئی من حیث كذا یعنی اس فن کا موضوع فلاں شے ہے بحیثیت فلاں۔ لفظ حیث یعنی حَیث کا لفظ اس کا وضعی معنی تو مکان کا ہے یعنی یہ ظرف مکان ہے اور اس مقام پر اس کا استعارہ کیا گیا ہے جہت شے اور اعتبار شے کے لئے يقال الموجود من حیث ھو موجود تو اس سے مراد ہوتی ہے کہ موجود موجود ہونے کی جہت و اعتبار کے ساتھ ملحوظ ہے موجودیت کے علاوہ اور کسی جہت کی لحاظ نہیں ہے ۔ والحيثية المذكورة فى الموضوع یعنی جو حیثیت مذکور فی الموضوع ہوتی ہے کبھی تو یہ ان اعراض میں داخل نہیں ہوتی جو اس علم میں مبحوث ہوتے ہیں۔ كقولهم موضوع العلم الإلهی الباحث لفظ الباحث علم الی کی صفت ہو کر بمنزلہ اس کی تعریف کے ہے یعنی علم الہی وہ علم ہے جو موجودات مجردہ کے احوال سے بحث کرتا ہے بایں حیثیت کہ وہ موجود ہے اور بیان لفظ مجردہ سے مراد مجرد عن القیود ہے یعنی موجودات مطلقہ اور مجردہ سے مراد مجرد عن المادة نہیں ہے کیونکہ علم الہی مطلق موجود سے بحث کرتا ہے خواہ مجرد عن المادہ ہو یا مقترن بالمادہ ہو تو یہاں اس حیثیت سے مراد یہ ہے کہ ہم ان احوال سے بحث کریں گے جو الموجود کو بحیثیت موجود ہونے کے لاحق ہوتے ہیں ۔ لا من حيث
It is not sufficient. Then after this, the commentator objected to the contradiction: that the author, in the examples he presented, contradicts himself because the subjects of usul al-fiqh are multiple things, and the predicates (issues) in usul al-fiqh are not accidents of the meaning of legal evidence but are accidents of the Book, Sunnah, consensus, etc. The commentator's objection is also frivolous because whatever the subject of a discipline is, its conceptual accidents are not discussed; rather, the accidents of its instances are discussed. For example, the word 'grammar' does not discuss the accidents of its meaning but discusses the accidents of its instances: noun, verb, particle. This does not necessitate that all these are called the subject. Similarly, the subject of usul al-fiqh is legal evidence; however, its four instances—Book, Sunnah, consensus, analogy—discussing their accidents is discussing the accidents of legal evidence, which is the subject of usul. Similarly, the subject of logic is 'that which leads to the unknown,' which is a single matter; then it is general whether this 'leading' is conceptual or propositional; in reality there is no multiplicity of subjects, but rather the subject is a single matter; however, its types are multiple. In any case, the author's research is praiseworthy; the truth is most worthy of being followed. The author said in al-Tawdih: 'And among them is that the aspect may be mentioned.' This is a second discussion because the subject of a discipline is concerned with some aspect; so this discussion relates to the investigation of this aspect. He said: 'It has two meanings'—i.e., two meanings can be intended by 'aspect.' First: That the thing concerned, together with this aspect, becomes the subject of the discipline, i.e., the aspect is also a part and component of the subject, just as the thing concerned is a part of the subject. As it is said: 'The existent qua existent is the subject of divine science.' So this aspect is included in the subject-status. In this case, the essential accidents that attach to the existent in that aspect of existence, such as unity and multiplicity, etc., are discussed, but the aspect itself is not discussed because in a science, the essential accidents of the subject are discussed; the very essence of the subject and its parts are not discussed. The famous dictum is that the subject of a discipline and its parts are considered settled in this science and are discussed in another science. Second: The second meaning of 'aspect' is that the aspect indicates the essential accidents that are discussed in the science, because the essential accidents of a thing are of different types, and in one science only one type of accidents of the subject is discussed, and the aspect indicates that type which is discussed. For example, their statement: 'The subject of medicine is the human body, in terms of health and illness,' and 'The subject of astronomy is the celestial bodies, in terms of shape.' Thus this aspect indicates the discussed essential accidents. The intended meaning is the second—i.e., it is an indication of the discussed accidents, not that it enters into the subject-status. That is why in medicine, health and illness themselves are discussed, and in astronomy, shape itself is discussed. If the first meaning were intended—i.e., if the aspect were part of the subject—then it would be necessary that in medicine and astronomy, those accidents that attach via those two mentioned aspects would be discussed, and the aspects themselves would not be discussed, but the reality is contrary to that, i.e., in actual fact it is not so; rather, these aspects themselves are discussed. The commentator said in al-Talwih: 'His statement: Among them is that the aspect may be mentioned.' This is a second discussion from the author, investigating the aspect that is mentioned with the subject of a discipline. For example, it is said: 'The subject of this science is such-and-such a thing under such-and-such an aspect.' The word 'hayth' literally means 'place,' i.e., it is an adverb of place, and here it is used metaphorically for the direction and consideration of a thing. It is said: 'The existent qua existent'—meaning that the existent is considered in terms of its existence, with no consideration of anything other than existence. And the aspect mentioned in the subject sometimes is not included among the accidents that are discussed in that science. For example, their statement: 'The subject of divine science is the investigator'—the word 'investigator' is an attribute of divine science, equivalent to its definition, i.e., divine science is that science which discusses the states of the separate existents, in the aspect that they exist. The expression 'separate' here means free from restrictions, i.e., absolute existents, and 'separate' does not mean free from matter, because divine science discusses absolute existence, whether it is separate from matter or associated with matter. So here this aspect means that we will discuss the states that attach to the existent qua existent, not from the aspect of...
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih addresses an important methodological issue: the role of 'haythiyya' (aspect/consideration) in defining the subject-matter of a discipline. The author begins by responding to an objection that usul al-fiqh has multiple subjects. He clarifies that the subject is actually one (legal evidence), and its four instances (Book, Sunnah, consensus, analogy) are not separate subjects; rather, discussing their accidents is still discussing the accidents of the one subject. He then moves to the main topic: the two meanings of 'haythiyya'. The first meaning treats the aspect as part of the subject itself; thus the essential accidents studied are those that accrue to the thing under that specific aspect, but the aspect itself is not studied in that science. The second meaning treats the aspect as a specification of which type of essential accidents are to be studied; here the aspect itself becomes the object of study. The commentator (Taftazani) confirms that the second meaning is correct, as seen in actual sciences like medicine and astronomy where the aspects (health/illness, shape) are themselves investigated. The passage provides a rigorous distinction that prevents confusion about what is studied in each science.

Key Terms
حیثیت (haythiyya): The aspect or consideration under which a subject is studied in a discipline; can have two meanings: as part of the subject or as an indication of the discussed accidents.
موضوع (mawdu'): The subject-matter of a science; that which is investigated in terms of its essential accidents.
اعراض ذاتیہ (a'rad dhatiyya): Essential accidents; properties that necessarily follow from the essence of the subject quâ that subject.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which meaning of 'haythiyya' is the intended one in defining a science like medicine?
A) The aspect is part of the subject, so the aspect itself is not discussed.
B) The aspect indicates the type of essential accidents discussed, and the aspect itself is discussed.
C) The aspect is the subject itself.
D) The aspect has no relation to the science.

Key Takeaway
The correct meaning of haythiyya in defining a science is the second: it specifies which essential accidents are studied, and the aspect itself is investigated.

The Two Types of Haythiyya in Defining a Science's Subject
Explaining the two types of haythiyya and the author's position on whether they are part of the subject

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of haythiyya in defining a science's subject and apply this distinction to classify whether a given aspect is part of the subject or an attribute to be discussed.

Core Idea
The haythiyya may be part of the subject (not discussed) or an aspect of the attributes discussed — a critical distinction for defining any science.

When defining the subject matter of a science, the phrase 'in terms of' (min haythu) introduces an aspect called haythiyya. Understanding whether that haythiyya is part of the subject or a description of the attributes to be discussed is essential for determining the scope of the science. This passage from the Hanafi usul tradition clarifies two types and addresses an objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and the haythiyya phrase
Look for the definition of a science's subject: e.g., 'the human body in terms of being healthy or sick.' The subject is 'human body' and the haythiyya is 'in terms of being healthy or sick.'
2
Determine whether the haythiyya is part of the subject or a description
According to the author, if the haythiyya is itself an attribute that the science discusses, it is the second type (description of a'rad dhatiyya). If it is merely a part of the subject, it is the first type and not discussed.
The first type: haythiyya is part of the subject, not discussed. The second type: haythiyya is a description of the attributes to be discussed, thus discussed.
3
Apply the author's rule to examples
For medicine: 'in terms of being healthy or sick' — health and sickness are discussed, so it is second type. For astronomy: 'in terms of having shape' — shape is discussed, so second type.
The author argues that if these aspects were parts of the subject, only their consequences would be discussed, not the aspects themselves.
4
Analyze the objection
An objector argues that both types of haythiyya should be considered conditions (qayd) for subjecthood, not parts. This would mean even the first type could be implicitly relevant.
The passage ends with the objection; no response is given here.
5
Evaluate the practical impact
The distinction affects which issues are considered part of the science's content. If a haythiyya is a part of the subject, it is not discussed; if it is a description of attributes, it is discussed directly.
This shapes the scope and boundaries of the discipline.

للموضوع قرار دیا جائے اور مصنف کے
Diverse types, i.e., they are of different kinds, and in a science, the discussion is about one kind of attributes of the subject, and the aspect (haythiyya) is a description of that kind being discussed. For example, their statement: 'The subject of medicine is the human body in terms of it being healthy and sick' and 'The subject of astronomy is the configuration of celestial bodies in terms of them having shape' – so this aspect is a description of those essential attributes being discussed. By it is meant the second meaning, i.e., it is a description of the discussed attributes, not that it is a part of the subject entering into subjecthood. That is why in medicine, health and sickness themselves are discussed, and in astronomy, shape is discussed. So if the first were meant, i.e., if this aspect were a part of the subject, then it would have been necessary that in medicine and astronomy, the attributes that occur by means of those two mentioned aspects would be discussed, and the aspects themselves would not be discussed – but the reality is contrary to that, i.e., in actual fact it is not so; rather, the aspects themselves are discussed. The commentator said in al-Talwih: 'His statement: Among them is that the aspect may be mentioned.' This is a second section from the author in which the investigation of the aspect that is mentioned with the subject of the discipline is conducted. For example, it is said: 'The subject of this science is such-and-such thing in terms of such-and-such aspect.' The word 'hayth' (where) literally means place, i.e., it is an adverb of place, and here it is metaphorically used for the consideration and viewpoint of a thing. It is said: 'Existence in terms of it being existence' – by this it is meant that existence is considered with the viewpoint and consideration of existence, and no other viewpoint aside from existence is considered. The mentioned aspect in the subject is sometimes not among the attributes that are discussed in that science, as in their statement: 'The subject of divine science is ...' The word 'al-bahith' (the investigator) is an attribute of divine science, serving as its definition, i.e., divine science is the science that discusses the states of abstract existents in terms of them existing. By the word 'abstract' is meant free from restrictions, not free from matter, because divine science discusses absolute existence, whether free from matter or associated with matter. So here, by this aspect it is meant that we will discuss the states that apply to existence in terms of it being existence, not in terms of... i.e., we will not discuss those states that apply to existence in terms of it being a substance or accident or body, or in terms of being free from matter. Rather, we will discuss states that apply in terms of existence. Like causality and being caused, or being necessary and possible, being eternal and originated – all these states apply to existence in terms of its existence. And the aspect of existence itself cannot be discussed because it has no meaning to affirm existence for existence and make it apply to existence in terms of being existence, otherwise the self-application of a thing by itself would be necessary. And it may be among the discussed attributes. This is the second type of aspect, i.e., the mentioned aspect together with the subject is sometimes among the attributes discussed in the science, i.e., the aspect itself is brought under discussion and its affirmation for the subject is made. As in their statement: 'The subject of medicine is the human body in terms of it being healthy or sick' or that the subject of natural science is body in terms of it moving and being at rest. So the science of medicine discusses health and sickness – thus this aspect is itself included among the discussed attributes. So the author's position is that the first type of aspect is a part of the subject, therefore due to being a part of the subject it is not discussed. The second type, i.e., the aspect that is discussed in the science, is not a part of the subject; rather, it is a description of the essential attributes discussed in the science. So it is included among the discussed attributes and hence is discussed. If this second type were also a part of the subject, like the first type, then discussing it in the science would not be correct, and making it a predicate of the issues would not be correct because in a science, the parts of the subject are not discussed; rather, its essential attributes are discussed. There is an objection against the author: Summary: We do not accept that in the first case the aspect is a part of the subject; rather, it is a condition for subjecthood, meaning the discussion is – the meaning of being a condition is clarified: that is, only those attributes that apply to the subject in that aspect and consideration will be discussed. So in the same way, in the second type, the aspect should also be considered a condition for the subject, and the author's...
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion from a Hanafi usul commentary about the concept of 'haythiyya' (aspect/perspective) in defining the subject matter of a science. The author distinguishes two types of haythiyya: (1) those that are part of the subject itself and thus not discussed in the science, and (2) those that are accidental attributes (a'rad) that the science actually investigates. The example from medicine and astronomy illustrates that the aspect 'in terms of health/sickness' or 'in terms of shape' is not part of the subject but describes what is discussed. The commentator from al-Talwih expands on the linguistic and conceptual basis of 'hayth'. An objection is raised: some argue that even the first type should be seen as a condition (qayd) of subjecthood rather than a part of the subject, which would affect whether it is discussed. The passage follows the author's own structure: first explaining the general meaning, then presenting the two types with examples, then clarifying the author's position and the objection. All examples are drawn from traditional Islamic sciences.

Key Terms
الحيثية (al-haythiyya): The aspect or perspective from which a thing is considered; in defining a science's subject, it specifies which attributes of the subject are to be discussed.
الاعراض الذاتية (al-a'rad al-dhatiyya): Essential accidents or attributes that necessarily follow from the subject's definition and are discussed in a science.
الموضوع (al-mawdu'): The subject matter of a science, defined by a specific aspect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, which type of haythiyya is part of the subject and therefore not discussed in the science?
A) The first type
B) The second type
C) Both types
D) Neither type

Key Takeaway
The haythiyya in a science's definition may either be a part of the subject (not discussed) or a description of the attributes to be discussed; this distinction shapes the scope of the discipline.
